Why Proper Nouns Are Not Mushtaqq in the Theoretical Sense
Defines the status of proper nouns regarding derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a proper noun (ʿalam) is not considered derived (mushtaqq) in the theoretical sense and identify the condition that must be met for derivation.

Core Idea
A proper noun indicates a specific entity, so it lacks the meaning correspondence required for derivation.

In Hanafi usul, derivation (ishtiqaq) requires a shared meaning between the derived word and its root. Proper nouns, when used as names, point to a specific person or thing directly, severing that semantic link. However, many proper nouns originally had a meaning (e.g., Hātim from 'to break'), and from that original perspective, they can be considered derived. This distinction clarifies how words are categorized as common nouns or proper nouns.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for derivation
Ishtiqaq (derivation) requires munāsaba fī al-maʿnā (correspondence in meaning) between the derived form and its root. Without this semantic link, a word cannot be considered derived in the technical sense.
This condition is central to Hanafi classification of words.
2
Identify what a proper noun indicates
A proper noun (ʿalam) indicates the specific entity (dhāt al-musammā) itself, not a meaning or attribute. For example, 'Zaid' names a person, not the concept of increase.
This direct reference is the key difference from common nouns.
3
Analyze why the condition fails from the theoretical (ʿilmī) perspective
Since a proper noun points directly to an entity, there is no shared meaning to connect it to a root. Thus, from the theoretical perspective, it is not mushtaqq—even if it has an etymological origin.
The perspective matters: current usage vs. historical origin.
4
Recognize that from the original meaning perspective it can be derived
When we consider the original meaning (manqūl ʿanhu) before it became a name, the word may be derived. In that case, it falls under ism jins (common noun) because the derivation is based on meaning, not entity.
This dual classification is unique to Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
در مخفی ان العلم یعنی علم باعتبار معنی علمی کے مشتق نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ اس معنی کے لحاظ سے علم دال على ذات المسمیٰ ہوتا ہے تو اس وقت مناسبة في المعنی جو شرط اشتقاق ہے معتبر نہیں ہو سکتی البتہ معنی اصل جو کہ منقول عنہ ہے اس معنی کے لحاظ سے علم مشتق ہو سکتا ہے تو اس وقت معنی اصلی کے لحاظ سے یہ اسم جنس میں داخل ہو جائیں گے تو مشتق حقیقتاً اسم جنس ہی ہوا اور مصنف کی مراد بھی یہی ہوگی کہ علم باعتبار معنی اصلی کے مشتق ہوتا ہے۔
It is hidden that: 'ilm (proper noun) cannot be derived from the 'ilmi (theoretical) perspective, because from this perspective, a proper noun indicates the specific entity (dhāt al-musammā). Then the condition of correspondence in meaning, which is a condition for derivation, cannot be considered. However, from the perspective of the original meaning (manqūl ‘anhu), a proper noun can be derived. In that case, from the perspective of the original meaning, it would be included among common nouns, so the derived word is in reality a common noun. And this is what the author intends: that a proper noun is derived by considering its original meaning.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a proper noun (ʿalam) can be considered mushtaqq (derived). It explains that in the theoretical (ʿilmī) sense, a proper noun cannot be derived because it directly indicates a specific entity (e.g., a person named Zaid), and the condition of meaning correspondence (munāsaba fī al-maʿnā) between the derived word and its root is not present. For example, the name Zaid is not derived from ziyādah (increase) in a direct semantic sense. However, with respect to its original meaning (the meaning before being used as a name), a proper noun can be derived. For instance, the name Hātim is originally from hatama (to break). In that sense, it falls under ism jins. So ultimately, a proper noun can be considered derived only if one looks at its etymological origin, and then it is essentially a common noun. This distinction is important in Hanafi usul for determining whether a word has a shabah (similarity) to a sifah (attribute), which affects categorization.

Key Terms
علم (ʿalam): A proper noun that indicates a specific person or thing.
اشتقاق (ishtiqāq): Derivation; the process of forming a word from a root with shared meaning.
مناسبة في المعنى (munāsaba fī al-maʿnā): Correspondence in meaning between derived word and root.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it indicates a specific entity, lacking meaning correspondence
C) Because it is always jamid
D) Because derivation only applies to verbs

Key Takeaway
A proper noun is not derived in its current function as a name, but can be derived when considering its original meaning, thus it is essentially a common noun in that perspective.

Refining Nakirah and Ma'rifah Definitions
Correcting the definitions of indefinite and definite nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of nakirah and ma'rifah based on coinage (wad') and distinguish them from context-based definitions.

Core Idea
Nakirah and ma'rifah are defined by the coiner's intention, not by context or hearer's knowledge.

In this passage, the commentator refines the definitions of nakirah and ma'rifah to cover all types. The earlier division based on 'specified individuals' was incomplete. The author provides a more comprehensive definition grounded in the concept of wad' (coinage). Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting general (amm) and specific (khass) texts in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with the earlier division
The earlier division only covered nakirah used for unspecified individuals and ma'rifah as 'ma'hood' (external definite). It excluded nakirah used for the essence and other definites like pronouns.
2
Understand the author's general definition
The author defines nakirah as 'coined for a thing not specified' and ma'rifah as 'coined for a specified meaning at the time of absolute usage.' This aims to include all types.
Note the phrase 'at the time of absolute usage for the hearer' is key.
3
Analyze the contested phrase
The phrase 'at the time of absolute usage for the hearer' could be misinterpreted to mean that usage or hearer determines definiteness. The commentator clarifies that it refers to the coiner's consideration at the time of coinage.
The word 'wad'' (coinage) is central.
4
Examine the objection and response
Objection: If a speaker says 'Jā'ani rajul', the word 'rajul' might be definite for the hearer, yet it remains nakirah. Response: The definition is about coinage, not context. The coiner does not intend specification for the hearer in nakirah.
This shows external factors do not change the category.
5
Conclude why the author's definition is superior
The author's definition covers all cases by focusing on the coiner's intention. It resolves the incompleteness of earlier divisions and withstands objections based on context.
This definition is consistent with Hanafi usul methodology.

وار فهى ما وضع لما كان الخارج الى الحاصل من التقسیم چونکہ مصنف نے تقسیم کے دوران کہا کہ بعض افراد معین مراد ہوں تو اس کو معہود یعنی معہود خارجی کہتے ہیں اور بعض افراد ملکر یعنی لا علی التعیین مراد ہوں تو نکرہ کہتے ہیں تو اس تقسیم جو کچھ خارج اور برامد ہوا ہے یہ بعض انواع نکرہ اور معرفہ میں یہ تقسیم جمیع انواع کو اپنے اندر مندرج نہیں کر رہی اب وہ نکرہ جو مستعمل في الفرد نہیں بلکہ نفس مسمیٰ میں مستعمل ہے وہ اس تقسیم میں درج نہیں ہورہا اور اسی طرح معرفہ کے اندر معہور آیا ہے اور باقی اقسام اس کے اندر درج نہیں ہو رہے تھے اس لئے مصنف نے نکرہ کی عمومی تعریف کی ما وضع لشئ الا بعینہ اب یہ کشتی لا بعینه خواه فرد ہو یا مفہوم جس میں کوئی تعیین مقصود نہ ہو اسی طرح معرفہ وہ ہوتا ہے جس کی وضع لمعنی معين عند الاطلاق ہوتا کہ جمیع اقسام نکرہ اور معرفہ کو شامل ہو جائے ۔
قوله عند الاطلاق للسامع تيدان احدهما الاطلاق وثانيهما اللسامع للتعيين في المعرفه وعدمة في النكرة والاحسن یعنی مصنف کی تعریف سے یہ تعریف که ان المعرفة الم يعنى معرفه وہ ہوتا ہے جس کی وضع اس لئے کی جائے کہ اس کی استعمال کسی معین شئی میں ہو اور تکرہ وہ اسم ہے جس کی وضع اس خاطر ہو کہ اس کی استعمال شی غیر معین میں ہونا لمعت ہو یعنی معرفہ اور نکرہ میں تعیین اور عدم تعیین کا اعتبار صرف دلالت لفظ اور وضع کے لحاظ سے ہوتا ہے ولا عبرة یعنی اس میں وضع کے علاوہ حالتہ اطلاق کا اعتبار نہیں ہوتا اور اسی طرح متکلم کے علاوہ صرف سامع کا اعتبار کیا جائے یوں بھی نہیں ہوتا لانه اذا قال یعنی کوئی شخص یہ قول کرے جانی رجل تو ہو سکتا ہے کہ یہ رجل کسی وجہ سے سامع کے نزدیک بھی متعین ہو جیسے متکلم کے لئے ممکن ہے تو بھی نکرہ ہوگا کیونکہ یہ تعین باعتبار دلالہ تو لفظ کے نہیں ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ حالة اطلاق اور للسامع وغیرہ کا معرفہ کی تعیین اور نکرہ میں عدم تعیین کے متعلق کوئی دخل نہیں ہے جواب یہ دیا جاتا ہے کہ مصنف کی مراد بھی عند الاطلاق للسامع سے یہی استعمال ہے تو مصنف کی تعریف کا خلاصہ یہ ہوا کہ معرفہ وہ ہوتا ہے جس کی وضع میں واضع کے نزدیک اس کی تعیین عند السا مع معتبر ہوتی ہے حال الاستعمال اور نکرہ کی وضع ایسی شئی کے لئے ہوتی ہے کہ واضع کے نزدیک اس کی تعيين للسامع بوقت استعمال معتبر نہیں ہوتی تو مصنف کی تعریف احسن ہے۔ قولہ واعلم انه يجب يريد یعنی مصنف کا مقصد یہ ہے کہ ان اقسام مذکورہ کا ایک
And it is that which is coined for what has come out from the division. Since the author said during the division that if some specific individuals are intended, it is called ma'hood (external definite), and if some individuals collectively, i.e., unspecified, are intended, it is called nakirah. Then what has come out from this division is that it does not cover all types of nakirah and ma'rifah. Now that nakirah which is not used for an individual but for the essence itself is not included in this division. Similarly, among ma'rifah only ma'hood came, and the remaining types were not included. Therefore, the author gave a general definition of nakirah: 'coined for a thing not specified'. Now this encompasses whatever is not specified, whether an individual or a concept, in which no specification is intended. Similarly, ma'rifah is that which is coined for a specified meaning at the time of absolute usage, so that all types of nakirah and ma'rifah are included.
His statement: 'at the time of absolute usage for the hearer' — two points: one is 'absolute usage', the other is 'for the hearer' for specification in ma'rifah and its absence in nakirah, and the better [definition] is... i.e., from the author's definition, this definition: that ma'rifah is that which is coined so that its usage is in a specified thing, and nakirah is a noun coined so that its usage is intended for an unspecified thing. That is, specification and non-specification in ma'rifah and nakirah are considered only from the perspective of the word's indication and coinage. 'And no consideration' means that beyond coinage, the state of absolute usage is not considered, and likewise, the hearer alone is not considered, aside from the speaker. Because if someone says, 'Jā'ani rajul' (a man came to me), it is possible that this 'rajul' becomes specified for the hearer for some reason, just as it may be for the speaker, yet it will still be nakirah because this specification is not due to the word's indication. So it becomes clear that the state of absolute usage and 'for the hearer' etc. have no role in the specification of ma'rifah and non-specification of nakirah. The response is given that the author's intent with 'at the time of absolute usage for the hearer' is also this usage. So the summary of the author's definition is: ma'rifah is that in whose coinage its specification for the hearer at the time of use is considered by the coiner; and nakirah is coined for a thing such that its specification for the hearer at the time of use is not considered by the coiner. Thus the author's definition is superior. His statement: 'And know that it is necessary' — the author intends to say that these mentioned types...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage refines the definitions of nakirah (indefinite) and ma'rifah (definite) in Arabic grammar and usul. The author of the matn had earlier divided nouns based on whether specific individuals are intended (ma'hood) or unspecified (nakirah), but that division did not cover all types (e.g., nakirah used for the essence, or other definites like pronouns and proper nouns). To remedy, the author provides a broader definition: nakirah is coined for a thing without specification; ma'rifah is coined for a specified meaning at the time of absolute usage. However, the phrase 'at the time of absolute usage for the hearer' is contested: some object that specification should be based solely on coinage, not usage or hearer. The commentator defends the author, explaining that 'at the time of absolute usage for the hearer' means that the coiner considers whether the noun is specified for the hearer when used absolutely. This is a key distinction: a word may be specified in context but still be nakirah if not coined for specification. The author's definition is thus superior because it accounts for the coiner's intention, not external factors.

Key Terms
نکرہ (nakirah): A noun coined for a thing not specified, whether individual or concept.
معرفہ (ma'rifah): A noun coined for a meaning specified at the time of absolute usage.
وضع (wad'): The act of coining a word for a meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's refined definition, what makes a noun ma'rifah?
A) It is used for a specific individual in context
B) The speaker intends a specific referent
C) The coiner considers it specified for the hearer at the time of absolute usage
D) It is commonly known among people

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of nakirah and ma'rifah is based on the coiner's intention at the time of coinage, not on context or hearer's knowledge.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Hanafi Definitions of Nakirah and Ma'rifah: Original Placement Over Context
Defining and defending the Hanafi definitions of nakirah and ma'rifah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of nakirah and ma'rifah based on original placement (wad') and distinguish them from definitions based on context.

Core Idea
Nakirah and ma'rifah are defined by the word's original placement, not by current context.

The commentator explains why a comprehensive definition was needed: the earlier division only covered some types. He then presents the author's definitions for nakirah and ma'rifah and defends them against a potential misinterpretation. This lesson unpacks those definitions and the key principle that categorization depends on the word's original placement (wad'), not on the listener's knowledge or the speaker's intention in a particular instance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with the earlier division
The earlier division only covered specific types of nakirah and ma'rifah (e.g., 'ahd for ma'rifah, unspecified individuals for nakirah). It missed other types like nakirah used for the essence itself and other ma'rifat such as pronouns. This motivated the author to give a comprehensive definition.
2
State the author's definitions
Nakirah: 'what is placed for something without specification (la bi-aynih)'. Ma'rifah: 'what is placed for a specific meaning at the time of discharge (at al-itlaq)'. These definitions encompass all types by focusing on the original placement.
Key phrase: 'at the time of discharge' refers to the moment the word is used, but the specification is built into the placement.
3
Examine the objection about 'at the time of utterance' and 'for the listener'
Some might think the author meant that the word becomes ma'rifah only if the listener actually knows the referent at the time of utterance. The commentator clarifies that the author's intent is that the placement itself considers the listener, not the actual context.
4
Analyze the example 'ra'aytu rajulan'
Even if both speaker and listener know which man is meant, the word 'rajulan' remains nakirah because its specification is not derived from the word's indication. This shows that external context does not change the category.
The specification must be from the word itself (bi i'tibar dalalat al-lafz) for it to be ma'rifah.
5
Summarize the Hanafi principle
The category (nakirah or ma'rifah) is determined solely by the original placement (wad') and the intention of the speaker when placing the word, not by the listener's knowledge or the circumstances of the utterance.
This principle is foundational for interpreting general and specific texts.

And this is what was established from the division. Since the author said during the division that if particular individuals are meant, it is called 'ahd, i.e., 'ahd khariji, and if individuals are meant without specification, it is called nakirah. So this division that has emerged is only some types of nakirah and ma'rifah; it does not encompass all types. Now that nakirah which is not used for an individual but for the essence itself (musamma) is not included in this division. Similarly, among ma'rifah, only 'ahd appears, and the remaining types are not included. Therefore, the author gave a general definition of nakirah: 'what is placed for something without specification (la bi-aynih)'. Now this includes both individual and meaning where no specification is intended. Similarly, ma'rifah is that which is placed for a specific meaning at the time of discharge (at the time of utterance), so that all types of nakirah and ma'rifah are included. His statement: 'at the time of utterance for the listener' has two indications: one is 'discharge' (at the time of utterance) and the second is 'for the listener' regarding specification in ma'rifah and its absence in nakirah. And the better (definition) is this definition: 'Ma'rifah is that which is placed so that it is used for a specific thing, and nakirah is that name which is placed so that it is used for a non-specific thing.' That is, in ma'rifah and nakirah, specification and non-specification are considered only from the perspective of the indication of the word and its (original) placement. 'And no consideration' means: besides placement, the state of discharge is not considered. Similarly, only the listener is considered, not the speaker; even this is not so. Because if a person says 'ra'aytu rajulan' (I saw a man), it is possible that this 'rajul' may be specified in the listener's mind for some reason, just as for the speaker, yet it will still be nakirah because this specification is not by virtue of the indication of the word. So it is known that the state of discharge and 'for the listener' etc. have no bearing on the specification of ma'rifah and the non-specification of nakirah. The answer given is that the author's intent by 'at the time of utterance for the listener' is also this usage. So the summary of the author's definition is that ma'rifah is that whose placement the speaker considers its specification for the listener at the time of use, and nakirah is placed for something such that the speaker does not consider its specification for the listener at the time of use. So the author's definition is superior. His statement: 'Know that it is necessary' means the author's aim is that these mentioned types are distinguished from each other not by essence but by aspects and considerations. And the two aspects that cause differentiation are sometimes not opposed to each other: like placing for many (katheer) (which is considered in mushtarak) and placing for individuals of a single meaning (which occurs in 'amm) — these two aspects are not contradictory. For example, the word 'uyun': from one aspect, it is placed for all individuals of 'al-ain al-jariyah' (flowing spring) by a single placement, so it is 'amm (general); and from another aspect, it is placed for multiple meanings: 'ain jariyah, 'ain basirah (seeing eye), shams (sun), dhahab (gold), etc., by multiple placements, so it is mushtarak (homonym). Thus, in this case, in one word, 'amm and mushtarak are combined due to different aspects. And sometimes those two aspects that cause differentiation are mutually opposed; they cannot be combined in one word, like placement for many non-enumerated (ghayr mahsur) (which occurs in 'amm) and placement for one or for many enumerated (like in khas or numbers). These two aspects are opposed, so a word cannot be both 'amm and khas from one aspect because these two opposing aspects cannot be combined in one word. As for what has been mentioned that nakirah mawsufah is 'amm from one aspect and khas from another, so in one word 'amm and khas are combined — the negation of combination you (the objector) made is not correct. The answer: 'The speech is still open to objection.' That is, despite the extreme effort in presenting this division and clarifying the types, still the author's speech is open to objection. Since the commentator (sharih) has not presented any objection from his side, there is no need for an answer to an objection. The chapter on 'khas' up to which was the syllabus of the unified schools along with al-Talwih is completed, by the grace of Allah. After this, only the commentary of al-Tawdih up to al-Muqaddimat al-Arba' (the Four Premises) will be explained.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definitions of nakirah (indefinite noun) and ma'rifah (definite noun) from a Hanafi usul work. The commentator begins by discussing a previous division (taksim) of these categories that was incomplete. The earlier division only covered some types: 'ahd (a previously mentioned specific individual) for ma'rifah and nakirah for unspecified individuals. This left out nakirah used for the essence itself (e.g., 'man' in the sense of 'humanity') and other types of ma'rifah like pronouns or proper names. Therefore, the original author (mussannif) provided a comprehensive definition: nakirah is a noun placed for a thing without specification (la bi-aynih), meaning it does not specify a particular individual or concept. Ma'rifah is a noun placed for a specific meaning at the time of utterance (at al-itlaq), regardless of whether the listener knows it or not. The commentator defends this definition against a potential objection: some might think that 'at the time of utterance' and 'for the listener' refer to actual contextual use, but the author means that the original placement (wad') itself intends specification or non-specification for the listener when the word is used. This is purely based on the linguistic convention (wad'), not on the speaker's actual intention or the listener's knowledge. For example, 'rajul' (man) is nakirah even if both speaker and listener know which man is meant, because the word itself does not denote a specific individual. Then, the passage introduces an important usul principle: the distinction between types like 'amm (general) and mushtarak (homonym) is not by essence but by aspect (haythiyya). A single word can be both 'amm and mushtarak from different perspectives. The example is 'uyun' (eyes/springs): as the plural of 'spring', it is 'amm (includes all springs); as a homonym, it has multiple meanings (springs, eyes, sun, gold, etc.). Conversely, 'amm and khas (specific) are defined by opposing aspects: 'amm is for an unlimited number (kathir ghayr mahsur), while khas is for one or a limited number (kathir mahsur). These cannot combine in the same word from the same aspect. The commentator then addresses an objection: if nakirah mawsufah (indefinite with an adjective) is both 'amm (because it doesn't specify an individual) and khas (because the adjective restricts it), this would contradict the earlier claim of impossibility. The response is that the objection is not valid because the word's being 'amm or khas depends on the aspect of the word itself, not on external qualification. Finally, the commentator notes that despite the effort, the author's presentation is still open to criticism, but since no specific criticism is given, no response is needed. The chapter on 'khas' ends here, and the next section will comment on al-Tawdih regarding the 'Four Premises'.

Key Terms
النكرة (an-nakirah): A noun placed for a thing without specification (la bi-aynih) — it does not specify a particular individual or concept.
المعرفة (al-ma'rifah): A noun placed for a specific meaning at the time of discharge (at al-itlaq), i.e., its original placement intends specification for the listener.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the word 'rajul' in 'ra'aytu rajulan' still nakirah even if the listener knows which man is meant?
A) Because the speaker did not intend to specify the man.
B) Because the word itself does not indicate a specific individual.
C) Because the listener might not actually know the man.
D) Because 'rajul' is a common noun and can never be definite.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of nakirah and ma'rifah rests on original placement (wad'), not context, ensuring consistent interpretation of general texts.

Aspectual Distinctions: Combining 'Amm, Khas, and Mushtarak in One Word
Explaining aspectual distinctions between 'amm, khas, and mushtarak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when 'amm and mushtarak can combine in one word and when 'amm and khas cannot, using the concept of aspect (haythiyya).

Core Idea
A single word can be both 'amm and mushtarak by different aspects, but 'amm and kas cannot combine from the same aspect.

This lesson delves into a subtle but critical usul principle: the distinction between general ('amm), specific (khas), and homonymous (mushtarak) words is by aspect (haythiyya), not by essence. The commentator uses the example of 'uyun (eyes/springs) to show how one word can be both 'amm and mushtarak from different angles. Conversely, 'amm and khas are defined by opposing aspects and cannot merge in the same word. The lesson concludes by addressing an objection regarding nakirah mawsufah.

Guided Path
1
State the commentator's opening claim
The commentator asserts that the different types (e.g., 'amm, khas, mushtarak) are distinguished not by their essence but by aspects (haythiyyat). This means a single word can belong to multiple categories depending on which aspect is considered.
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul for understanding polysemy and generality.
2
Identify the two types of aspect relationships
Some aspects are not opposed and can coexist in one word. Examples: the aspect of placing for many (kathir) as in mushtarak, and placing for all individuals of a single meaning as in 'amm. These can combine. Other aspects are opposed: e.g., placing for many non-enumerated (ghayr mahsur) as in 'amm vs. placing for one or many enumerated as in khas. These cannot combine.
The opposition is because 'amm requires unlimited scope, while khas requires limitation.
3
Analyze the example of 'uyun'
The word 'uyun' from one aspect is placed for all individuals of 'al-ain al-jariyah' (flowing spring) by a single placement — it is 'amm. From another aspect, it is placed for multiple distinct meanings (spring, eye, sun, gold, etc.) by multiple placements — it is mushtarak. Thus, 'uyun is both 'amm and mushtarak by different aspects.
4
Apply the principle to nakirah mawsufah
Some object: nakirah mawsufah (e.g., 'rajulun salihun' - a righteous man) appears to be both 'amm (the nakirah alone) and khas (due to the adjective). The commentator argues that this does not combine 'amm and khas from the same aspect; the nakirah's generality is from its own meaning, while the specificity comes from the adjective, which is external. Hence, no contradiction.
The objection is dismissed because the combination is not within the same word from the same aspect.
5
Explain why the author's speech is still open to objection
The commentator notes that despite the effort in the division, the author's presentation remains open to criticism. However, since no specific criticism is mentioned, no response is needed. This leaves room for further scholarly debate.
This shows the commentator's humility and recognition of the limits of any single exposition.

And this is what was established from the division. Since the author said during the division that if particular individuals are meant, it is called 'ahd, i.e., 'ahd khariji, and if individuals are meant without specification, it is called nakirah. So this division that has emerged is only some types of nakirah and ma'rifah; it does not encompass all types. Now that nakirah which is not used for an individual but for the essence itself (musamma) is not included in this division. Similarly, among ma'rifah, only 'ahd appears, and the remaining types are not included. Therefore, the author gave a general definition of nakirah: 'what is placed for something without specification (la bi-aynih)'. Now this includes both individual and meaning where no specification is intended. Similarly, ma'rifah is that which is placed for a specific meaning at the time of discharge (at the time of utterance), so that all types of nakirah and ma'rifah are included. His statement: 'at the time of utterance for the listener' has two indications: one is 'discharge' (at the time of utterance) and the second is 'for the listener' regarding specification in ma'rifah and its absence in nakirah. And the better (definition) is this definition: 'Ma'rifah is that which is placed so that it is used for a specific thing, and nakirah is that name which is placed so that it is used for a non-specific thing.' That is, in ma'rifah and nakirah, specification and non-specification are considered only from the perspective of the indication of the word and its (original) placement. 'And no consideration' means: besides placement, the state of discharge is not considered. Similarly, only the listener is considered, not the speaker; even this is not so. Because if a person says 'ra'aytu rajulan' (I saw a man), it is possible that this 'rajul' may be specified in the listener's mind for some reason, just as for the speaker, yet it will still be nakirah because this specification is not by virtue of the indication of the word. So it is known that the state of discharge and 'for the listener' etc. have no bearing on the specification of ma'rifah and the non-specification of nakirah. The answer given is that the author's intent by 'at the time of utterance for the listener' is also this usage. So the summary of the author's definition is that ma'rifah is that whose placement the speaker considers its specification for the listener at the time of use, and nakirah is placed for something such that the speaker does not consider its specification for the listener at the time of use. So the author's definition is superior. His statement: 'Know that it is necessary' means the author's aim is that these mentioned types are distinguished from each other not by essence but by aspects and considerations. And the two aspects that cause differentiation are sometimes not opposed to each other: like placing for many (katheer) (which is considered in mushtarak) and placing for individuals of a single meaning (which occurs in 'amm) — these two aspects are not contradictory. For example, the word 'uyun': from one aspect, it is placed for all individuals of 'al-ain al-jariyah' (flowing spring) by a single placement, so it is 'amm (general); and from another aspect, it is placed for multiple meanings: 'ain jariyah, 'ain basirah (seeing eye), shams (sun), dhahab (gold), etc., by multiple placements, so it is mushtarak (homonym). Thus, in this case, in one word, 'amm and mushtarak are combined due to different aspects. And sometimes those two aspects that cause differentiation are mutually opposed; they cannot be combined in one word, like placement for many non-enumerated (ghayr mahsur) (which occurs in 'amm) and placement for one or for many enumerated (like in khas or numbers). These two aspects are opposed, so a word cannot be both 'amm and khas from one aspect because these two opposing aspects cannot be combined in one word. As for what has been mentioned that nakirah mawsufah is 'amm from one aspect and khas from another, so in one word 'amm and khas are combined — the negation of combination you (the objector) made is not correct. The answer: 'The speech is still open to objection.' That is, despite the extreme effort in presenting this division and clarifying the types, still the author's speech is open to objection. Since the commentator (sharih) has not presented any objection from his side, there is no need for an answer to an objection. The chapter on 'khas' up to which was the syllabus of the unified schools along with al-Talwih is completed, by the grace of Allah. After this, only the commentary of al-Tawdih up to al-Muqaddimat al-Arba' (the Four Premises) will be explained.

Commentary

Key Terms
العام (al-‘amm): A word placed by a single placement for all individuals of a single meaning without enumeration (ghayr mahsur).
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A word placed by multiple placements for multiple distinct meanings.
الخاص (al-khas): A word placed for one individual or for a limited, enumerated set (mahsur).

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why can a word be both 'amm and mushtarak but not both 'amm and khas?
A) Because 'amm and mushtarak both deal with multiple meanings, while khas deals with one.
B) Because the aspects defining 'amm and mushtarak are not opposed, while those of 'amm and khas are opposed.
C) Because 'amm is always stronger than mushtarak, so they can combine.
D) Because khas is a subset of 'amm, so they cannot be equal.

Key Takeaway
A word's classification as 'amm, khas, or mushtarak depends on the aspect considered; 'amm and mushtarak can coexist, but 'amm and khas cannot from the same aspect.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Combining 'Amm and Mushtarak in One Word
Textual analysis of how 'amm and mushtarak can combine in one word

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'amm and mushtarak and identify when a single word can carry both categories from different aspects.

Core Idea
A single word can be both 'amm and mushtarak when viewed from different aspects.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the categories of 'amm (general) and mushtarak (homonymous) are defined by the nature of their coinage (wad'). At first glance, they seem mutually exclusive, but this passage reveals that a word can belong to both categories simultaneously when considered from different perspectives. The example of 'uyun illustrates this nuanced understanding, which is essential for accurate textual analysis (istinbat).

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two aspects
The passage distinguishes between the aspect of 'amm (single wad' for all individuals of one meaning) and the aspect of mushtarak (multiple wad's for multiple distinct meanings). Understand that these aspects are not inherently contradictory.
Aspect (haythiyya) is the key conceptual tool.
2
Apply to the example 'uyun
Analyze the word 'uyun (plural of 'ayn) as the passage does: from one aspect, it is 'amm because it is coined for all individuals of 'flowing spring'; from another aspect, it is mushtarak because it also means 'seeing eye', 'sun', 'gold', etc.
The word 'uyun alone is ambiguous; the aspect determines the category.
3
Contrast with 'amm and khass
The passage clarifies that 'amm and khass cannot coexist in one word because their wad' principles are contradictory: 'amm is for unlimited individuals, khass for one or limited individuals. This is unlike the combination of 'amm and mushtarak.
Khass is defined by limitation (hashr), which opposes the generality of 'amm.
4
Evaluate the objection
The passage dismisses an objection about the indefinite described (nakirah mawsufah) being both 'amm and khass, noting that the commentator did not raise a valid specific objection. Reflect on the importance of precise terminology in usul al-fiqh.
This shows that seemingly contradictory categories can be reconciled through correct aspectual analysis.

دوسرے سے تمایز باعتبار ذات کے نہیں ہے بلکہ بلحاظ حیثیات و اعتبارات کے ہے اور اور حیثیتین جو باعث امتیاز ہیں کبھی ایک دوسرے کے ساتھ متنافی نہیں ہونگی کا لوضع الكثير جیسے معنی کثیر کے لئے وضع کثیر کا ہونا جو مشترک میں معتبر ہے اور معنی واحد کے افراد کیلئے وضع واحد کا ہوتا جیسے عام میں ہوتا ہے ان دو حیثیتیوں میں منافات نہیں جیسے لفظ عیون بایں حیثییت کہ العین المجاریہ یعنی بہنے والا چشمہ کے جمیع افراد کے لئے موضوع بوضع واحد ہے تو عام ہے اور اگر بائیں حیثیت دیکھیں کہ متعدد معانی عین جاریہ ۔ عین باصرہ اور شمس وذہب وغیرہ کے لئے موضوع باوضاع کثیرہ متعددہ ہے تو مشترک ہے تو اس صورت میں ایک ہی لفظ میں باختلاف حیثیتین عام اور مشترک جمع ہو گئے و قدرمتنانیان اور کبھی وہ حیثیتین جو موجب امتیاز ہیں وہ آپس میں متنافی ہوں گی ایک لفظ میں ان کا اجتماع نہیں ہو سکے گا جیسے وضع لکثیر غیر محصور جو عام میں ہوتی ہے اور وضع لواحد او لكثير محصور جیسے خاص میں ہوتی تھی اور عدد جس کی وضع کثیر محصور کے لئے وہ بھی خاص میں درج ہے یہ دونوں حیثیات آپس میں متنافین میں لہذا ایک لفظ باعتبار الحيتيتين عام اور خاص نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ یہ حیثیتین متنافیین ایک لفظ میں جمع نہیں ہو سکتیں دما ذکر اور جو یہ مذکور ہوا ہے کہ نکرہ موصوفہ من وجہ عام ہے اور من وجہ خاص ہے تو ایک ہی لفظ میں عام و خاص جمع ہو گئے آپ نے جو نفی اجتماع کی ہے وہ صحیح نہیں نیٹی جواب والكلام بعد موضع نظر یعنی با وجود اس کے کہ اس تقسیم کی تقریر اور تبیین اقسام کے بارہ میں انتہائی تکلف کرنے کے باوجود پھر بھی کلام مصنف محل نظر ہے ۔ چونکہ شارح نے کوئی نظر اپنی طرف سے پیش نہیں کی لہذا جواب نظر کی بھی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
The distinction from another is not by essence but by aspects and considerations. And the two aspects that cause distinction are sometimes not contradictory with each other, like wad' al-kathir: just as for multiple meanings there is multiple wad' which is considered in mushtarak, and for individuals of a single meaning there is a single wad' as in 'amm. There is no contradiction between these two aspects. For example, the word 'uyun: from the aspect that it is coined with a single wad' for all individuals of 'al-'ayn al-jariyah' (flowing spring), it is 'amm; and if you consider the aspect that it is coined with multiple wad's for multiple meanings like flowing spring, seeing eye, sun, gold etc., then it is mushtarak. Thus in this case, in one word, according to different aspects, 'amm and mushtarak are combined. And sometimes the two aspects that cause distinction are contradictory, so they cannot be combined in one word, like wad' for many unlimited individuals which occurs in 'amm, and wad' for one or many limited individuals which occurs in khass, and numbers which are coined for many limited individuals are also included in khass. These two aspects are contradictory, therefore a word cannot be both 'amm and khass according to two aspects, because these contradictory aspects cannot be combined in one word. And what was mentioned that the indefinite described is 'amm in one respect and khass in another respect, so 'amm and khass are combined in one word—you have negated the combination, that is not correct. The answer: the speech is after the point of consideration, i.e., despite great effort in presenting this division and explanation of types, the author's speech is still subject to scrutiny. Since the commentator did not present any objection of his own, there is no need for an answer to the objection.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis clarifying the relationship between 'amm (general) and mushtarak (homonymous) in the Hanafi usul framework. The author explains that a single word can be both 'amm and mushtarak if considered from different aspects (haythiyat). The example of 'uyun (plural of 'ayn) illustrates this: from one aspect it is 'amm because it applies to all individuals of the meaning 'flowing spring'; from another aspect it is mushtarak because it has multiple meanings. These two aspects are not contradictory, so coexistence is possible. However, 'amm and khass are contradictory because their wad' principles are fundamentally different: 'amm is coined for unlimited individuals, while khass is coined for one or limited individuals. Therefore, a word cannot be both 'amm and khass simultaneously from the same aspect. The author then addresses an objection about the indefinite described (nakirah mawsufah) being both 'amm and khass, but dismisses it, noting that the commentator did not raise any specific objection, so no response is required.

Key Terms
عام ('amm): A word coined for all individuals of a single meaning without limitation
مشترک (mushtarak): A word coined for multiple distinct meanings with separate wad's
حیثیت (haythiyya): A specific aspect or perspective from which a word is considered
وضع (wad'): The original coinage of a word for a meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul analysis, can a single word be both 'amm (general) and mushtarak (homonymous) simultaneously?
A) Yes, from different aspects.
B) No, they are mutually exclusive.
C) Yes, from the same aspect.
D) Only if the word is a proper noun.

Key Takeaway
A word can be both 'amm and mushtarak if considered from different aspects, but cannot be both 'amm and khass due to contradictory wad' principles.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Definitiveness of Khass and Its Application to Three Quru'
Defines khass as qat'i and applies it to Quranic verse on 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that khass is qat'i to derive the meaning of 'quru'' as hayd from the verse 'three quru'' and distinguish it from the Shafi'i interpretation.

Core Idea
The specific number 'three' in the Quran forces the interpretation of 'quru'' as menstrual periods because only then the count is exact.

In this lesson, we explore a key Hanafi usul principle: the definitiveness (qat'i) of a khass text. The word 'three' in the Quran is khass, so its meaning is binding with certainty. We will see how this principle is applied to the disputed word 'quru'' (menstruation or purity) in the verse on 'iddah. By tracing the Hanafi derivation, you will understand why the Hanafi school holds that 'quru'' means hayd, not tuhr.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of khass as such
Identify what 'khass as such' means: a word considered without contextual preventers. It necessitates a ruling (either linguistic or legal).
The author distinguishes between the linguistic khass (e.g., 'Zaid is knowledgeable') and legal khass (Quranic text).
2
Grasp the two meanings of qat'i
Recognize that qat'i can mean either 'no probability arising from evidence' or 'no probability at all'. The Hanafi position uses the first meaning: the text is clear unless overridden by strong evidence.
The second meaning (absolute certainty) is too strong; even a khass text could be overridden if evidence arises.
3
Identify the khass element in the verse
In 'three quru'' (ثلاثة قروء), the word 'three' is khass: it specifies a definite number. This is the key textual anchor.
The number 'three' leaves no ambiguity about the count.
4
Show how interpreting quru' as tuhr violates khass
If quru' meant periods of purity (tuhr), then the 'iddah would either count the first tuhr (which may be incomplete) plus two, making less than three, or count three tuhrs after that, exceeding the definite number. Both conflict with the khass 'three'.
Only by interpreting quru' as hayd does the number correspond exactly to three full menstrual cycles.
5
Conclude the Hanafi ruling
Therefore, the definitiveness of 'three' forces the interpretation of quru' as hayd. This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in calculating 'iddah.

Chapter on the specific (khass) as such. After finishing the divisions, these are several chapters concerning the rulings of categories outside the division. So it is said: 'khass as such' means the aspect that does not consider accidents and preventers, nor any context that prevents from intending the real meaning. After disregarding such matters, 'khass as such necessitates the ruling.' That is, the word khass makes the ruling binding. 'Ruling' may either mean the attribution of something to another, like 'Zaid is knowledgeable' (Zaid 'alim) – here 'Zaid' is khass for its meaning and referent, and similarly the word ''alim' is khass for its meaning, so this phrase only necessitates the meaning of knowledge exclusively for Zaid. Or 'ruling' may mean a legal ruling, because the discussion is about khass from the Book, so it necessitates a legal ruling. 'Qat'an' meaning khass necessitates a definite ruling. It is said: 'al-qat'i is intended with two meanings' – one: that it has no probability arising from evidence; two: that it has no probability at all, neither from evidence nor without evidence. The first meaning is general, and the second is specific. And here the intended meaning is the first, which is general. The root is that probability arising from evidence is absolutely specific, and absolute probability whether arising from evidence or without evidence is absolutely general. Because where the first exists, the second necessarily exists; where the second exists, the first does not necessarily exist, because in probability without evidence, absolute probability exists but probability arising from evidence does not. When negation is applied to them, they become contraries, and the contrary of the more specific becomes more general, and the contrary of the more general becomes more specific. Therefore, negating probability arising from evidence yields the general meaning, which is intended here. So in the verse of Allah: 'three quru'' (innama qur'u), the word 'three' is khass, which definitely includes its denotation. Therefore, in this verse, the word 'qur'' cannot be interpreted as 'tuhr' (purity), as is the madhhab of the Shafi'is; otherwise the requirement of the khass would not be fulfilled. As for the calculation: if divorce occurred during tuhr, that tuhr is counted in the 'iddah as per the Shafi'i school, and two others are counted, then the required would be two tuhrs and part of a tuhr, thus the requirement of 'three' is not fulfilled. If that tuhr is not counted but three tuhrs are required separately, then...
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis and ruling on the definitiveness of khass. It is part of a chapter discussing the rulings of categories outside the main division. The author defines 'khass as such' (al-khass min hayth huwa khass) as a word considered without any contextual preventers. Such a word necessitates a ruling either as a linguistic predication or as a legal ruling. The key point is that khass yields a qat'i (definitive) ruling, but 'qat'i' here means it has no probability arising from evidence (i.e., it is textually clear), not that it has no probability at all (which would exclude even unreasonable possibilities). This distinction is important. The author then applies this to the Quranic verse 'three quru'' (2:228). The word 'three' is khass, so it definitively means exactly three periods. The Hanafi position is that quru' means menstrual periods (hayd), not periods of purity (tuhr). If quru' were interpreted as tuhr (as the Shafi'is hold), the requirement of 'three' would either be violated or lead to an incomplete count. Hence, the khass nature of 'three' forces the interpretation as hayd, because only then does the number exactly correspond to three complete cycles. This is a classic Hanafi proof that quru' means hayd, based on the definitiveness of the number.

Key Terms
خاص (khass): A word coined for a specific meaning, either one individual or a limited number (محصور)
قطعی (qat'i): Definitive in meaning; here meaning no probability arising from evidence (not absolute absence of probability)
قرء (qur'): Menstrual period (حیض) according to Hanafis; period of purity (طهر) according to Shafi'is

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in the passage, why does the khass word 'three' in 'three quru'' require that 'quru'' mean menstruation (hayd) rather than purity (tuhr)?
A) Because 'three' is a number and cannot apply to periods of purity.
B) Because interpreting it as tuhr would result in less than three full periods or count an incomplete period.
C) Because the Shafi'i interpretation leads to contradiction with another verse.
D) Because the word 'quru'' is mushtarak and must be clarified by the number.

Key Takeaway
The definitiveness of a khass text (like a number) is a powerful tool in Hanafi usul that can resolve lexical ambiguity and determine the correct legal ruling.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Specific Term (Khas) Necessitates a Definitive Ruling: Application to 'Three Periods'
Applying the principle that a specific term yields a definitive ruling to the verse on 'iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a specific term (khas) yields a definitive ruling (qat'i) to resolve the interpretive dispute over 'qur'' in Quran 2:228.

Core Idea
A specific term demands exact fulfillment; any interpretation leading to excess or deficiency is invalid.

This passage delves into a core Hanafi usul principle: a specific term (khas) gives a definitive ruling (qat'i). It then applies this principle to a famous Quranic verse concerning the waiting period of divorced women. The interpretive dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the meaning of 'qur'' (menstruation vs. purity) becomes a test case for how a specific numerical term must be satisfied exactly. Understanding this application will sharpen your ability to evaluate how textual categories drive legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define 'khas' in Hanafi usul
Identify the characteristics of a specific term: it indicates its meaning without probability, excluding external factors or contexts that might prevent literal interpretation.
Hanafis hold that khas is qat'i ad-dalala (definitive in indication) until evidence for specification (takhsis) appears.
2
Locate the specific term in the verse
In Quran 2:228, the word 'ثلاثة' (three) is the specific term. It requires exactly three units of whatever 'قرء' refers to.
The word 'قروء' is the plural of 'قرء' and is ambiguous between menstruation and purity.
3
Test each meaning against the requirement of exactness
Assume 'قرء' means purity (tuhr) as per the Shafi'i school. Evaluate the two ways to count: include the purity in which divorce occurred or exclude it. Both produce either less than or more than three purities, violating the specific term. Then assume 'قرء' means menstruation (hayd); after the initial purity, exactly three menstruations follow without excess or deficiency.
This logical test shows that only the Hanafi interpretation satisfies the specific term.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the counter-objection
The objection arises when divorce occurs during menstruation (non-legislated). The Hanafis do not count that menstruation, potentially causing deficiency. Respond: (a) the verse addresses legislated divorce (in purity), and (b) if needed, compensation can come from a part of a fourth menstruation, though the Shari'ah does not divide a single menstruation.
This demonstrates how the principle is defended even in edge cases.

اصل بات یہ ہے کہ احتمال ناشی عن الدلیل خاص مطلق ہے اور مطلق احتمال سواء کان ناشيا عن الدليل اوبلا دلیل یہ عام مطلق ہے کیونکہ جہاں اول موجود ہو گا ثانی ضرور موجود ہوگا جہاں ثانی موجود ہو اول کا موجود ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے کیونکہ احتمال ناشی بلا دلیل میں مطلق احتمال تو موجود ہے لیکن ناشی عن الدلیل موجود نہیں ہے جب ان پر نفی عائد کی جائیگی تو یہ نقیضین ہو جائیگی اور نقیص اخص عام ہو جاتی ہے اور نقیض اعم خاص ہو جاتی ہے۔ لہذا نفی احتمال ناشی عن الدلیل یہ معنی عام ہو گا جو یہاں مراد ہے. ففي قوله تعالى ثلاثة قروء لا يحمل القرء على الطهر اتم جو خاص کا موجب قطعی قرار دیا ہے یہ اس پر چند تفریعات میں یہ تفریح اول ہے حاصل یہ کہ اس آیت میں لفظ ثلاثہ خاص ہے جو اپنے مدلول کو قطعا شامل ہے لہذا اس آیت میں لفظ قرء کو طہر کے معنی پر محمول نہیں کیا جائے گا کما ھو مذہب الشوافع ورنہ تو مقتضی خاص پورا نہیں ہوگا خان احتسب الطهر یعنی اگر وہ طہر میں طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے عدت میں اس کو شمار کیا جائے کما ھو منذ سب الشوافع اور اس کے علاوہ دوطر شمار کیے جائیں تو پھر واجب دو طہر اور بعض الظہر ہوئے ثلاثہ کا مقتضا پورانہ ہوا اگر اس ظہر کو شمار نہ کیا جائے بلکہ تین طہر اس کے علاوہ لازم ہوں تو پھر تین اور بعض المطهر واجب في العدة سوا تین کا مقتضیٰ پھر بھی پورا نہ ہوا پہلی صورت میں تین سے کم دوسری صورت میں ثلاثہ سے زائد لازم ہوئے بخلاف اس کے کہ اگر حیض مراد ہو تو طہر جس کے اندر طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے اس کے بعد تین حیض کی مقدار بغیر کسی کمی بیشی کے محقق ہو جاتی ہے۔ اعلم ان القدم لفظ مشترک یعنی لفظ قرء مشترک ہے اس کی وضع حیض کے لئے بھی ہے اور طہر کے لئے بھی ۔ اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول والمطلقات نیتربصن با نفمن ثلثہ قروء میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک قرء سے حیض مراد ہے اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک طہر مراد ہے نحن نقول ہم یعنی حنفیہ حضرات کہتے ہیں کہ اگر قریر سے مراد طہر ہو تو موجب خاص باطل ہو جاتا ہے کیونکہ لفظ ثلثہ خاص ہے اور طلاق مشروع وہ ہے جو حالت طہر میں واقع ہو نہ وہ جو حالت حیض میں ہو۔ فالطهر الذي طلق نیه یعنی وہ طہر جس میں طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے اگر اس کو عدت میں شمار نہ کیا جائے بلکہ تین طہر اس کے علاوہ اعتبار کئے جائیں تو یجب ثلاثة اطهار و بعض طهرین طہر اور بعض طہر واجب ہوگا تو یہ ثلاثہ کی تعداد سے زائد ہو جائیگا دان احتسب یعنی اس طہر کو جس میں طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے اگر اس کو عدت میں حساب کیا جائے جیسے کہ امام شافعی کا مذہب ہے کہ اس طہر کو عدت میں شمار کرتے ہیں تو یجب طهران وبعض رو طہر اور بعض حصہ طہر کا ہوا تو بھی مقدار ثلثہ پوری نہ ہوئی حالانکہ لفظ ثلاثہ خاص ہے جو اپنے حکم کا موجب قطعی ہے تو وہ حیض مراد لینے کی صورت میں ہو سکتا ہے لہذا ا اس قرر سے مراد حیض ہی ہوگا کہا ھو مذہب الاحناف البتہ احناف پر بھی اس قسم کا اشکال اس صورت میں واقع ہوتا جب کہ عورت کو حالت حیض میں طلاق دی جائے تو احناف اس حیض کو عدت میں شمار نہیں کرتے بلکہ اس کے علاوہ تین کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں تو اس صورت میں تین حیض اور بعض حیض ہو جائیگا جو زائد علی الثلثہ ہے تو اس صورت میں علی مذہب الاحناف بھی خاص کا موجب پورا نہیں ہوتا اولاً تو یہ جواب دیا جاتا ہے کہ کلام طلاق مشروع میں ہے کیونکہ شریعت کی نگاہ میں امر مشروع کے احکام کو پیش کرنا مقصود بالذات ہے اور غیر مشروع امر کے احکام اجماع وغیرہ سے مستفاد ہوں گے ثانیا یہ کہ مقتضائے ثلثہ تو پورا کرنا ضروری ہے تو اس کی خاطر کچھ ایام حیض رابع کے ملانے پڑیں گے تاکہ پہلا حیض جس میں طلاق واقع ہوتی ہے اُس کا نقص پورا کیا جائے لیکن شریعت کی نگاہ میں تجزیہ حیض واحد کا
Such as the section on the specific in terms of being specific. After finishing the divisions, these are a few sections that pertain to the rulings of categories excluded from the division. So it is said: the specific in terms of being specific — meaning the consideration is that there is no consideration of attributes and preventers, and there is no context that prevents from intending the real meaning. After disregarding such matters, the specific in its capacity as specific necessitates the ruling, i.e., the specific word makes the ruling obligatory. The intended meaning of 'ruling' is either the ascription of something to another, like 'Zaid is knowledgeable' — here 'Zaid' is specific for its meaning and referent, and similarly the word 'alim' is specific for its meaning. So this expression would make the meaning of knowledge obligatory only for Zaid. And it is also possible that 'ruling' means a legal ruling, because the discussion is about the specific from the Book, so it would be the cause of a legal ruling. 'Definitively': i.e., the specific is the cause of a definitive ruling. Know that 'definitive' has two meanings: one is that it contains no probability arising from evidence; the second is that it contains no probability at all, i.e., neither from evidence nor without evidence. The first meaning is general, and the second is specific. What is intended here is the first meaning, which is general. The real point is that probability arising from evidence is absolutely specific, and absolute probability — whether arising from evidence or without evidence — is absolutely general. Because where the first exists, the second necessarily exists; where the second exists, the first does not necessarily exist, because probability without evidence has absolute probability but does not have probability arising from evidence. When negation is applied to them, they become contradictories, and the contradictory of the specific becomes general, and the contradictory of the general becomes specific. Hence, negation of probability arising from evidence is a general meaning, which is intended here. So in the Almighty's saying 'three periods' (thalaathatu quroo'), the word 'qur'' is not interpreted as purity (tuhur), because this is the first of several corollaries on making the specific a cause of a definitive ruling. The gist is that in this verse the word 'three' is specific, which definitively includes its referent. Therefore, the word 'qur'' in this verse will not be interpreted as purity, as is the school of the Shafi'is. Otherwise, the requirement of the specific would not be fulfilled. 'If the purity is counted': i.e., if the purity in which divorce occurred is counted in the 'iddah, as is the school of the Shafi'is, and besides that two purities are counted, then two purities and part of a purity would be obligatory, so the requirement of three would not be fulfilled. If that purity is not counted, but rather three purities besides it are necessary, then three purities and part of a purity would be obligatory in the 'iddah, so again the requirement of three would not be fulfilled. In the first case, less than three; in the second, more than three. In contrast, if menstruation (hayd) is intended, then after the purity in which divorce occurred, the amount of three menstruations is realized without any increase or decrease. Know that the word 'qur'' is homonymous: it is placed for both menstruation and purity. In the Almighty's saying 'and divorced women shall wait by themselves for three periods,' according to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'qur'' means menstruation, and according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it means purity. We (the Hanafis) say: if 'qur'' is intended to mean purity, then the requirement of the specific would be invalidated, because the word 'three' is specific, and the legislated divorce is that which occurs in a state of purity, not that which occurs in menstruation. So the purity in which the divorce occurred: if it is not counted in the 'iddah, but rather three purities besides it are considered, then three purities and part of a purity would be obligatory, which exceeds the number three. And if it is counted, i.e., the purity in which divorce occurred is counted in the 'iddah, as is the school of al-Shafi'i that they count that purity, then two purities and part of a purity would be obligatory, and still the amount of three would not be fulfilled, even though the word 'three' is specific and is a definitive cause of its ruling. So it can only be that the intended meaning is menstruation, which is the school of the Hanafis. However, a similar objection arises for the Hanafis when a woman is divorced during menstruation: the Hanafis do not count that menstruation in the 'iddah, but rather count three beyond it. In that case, there would be three menstruations and part of a menstruation, which exceeds three. So in that case, even according to the Hanafi school, the requirement of the specific would not be fulfilled. The first response is that the discussion concerns legislated divorce, because the Shari'ah's focus is on presenting the rulings of a legislated act, and the rulings of a non-legislated act are derived from consensus etc. The second is that it is necessary to fulfill the requirement of three, so for that purpose some days of a fourth menstruation would be added to compensate for the deficiency of the first menstruation in which divorce occurred. But in the view of the Shari'ah, a single menstruation cannot be divided.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that expands on the principle that a specific term (khas) gives a definitive ruling (qat'i). It begins by defining the term 'specific' as excluding external factors and contexts that might deter from the literal meaning. The author distinguishes two meanings of 'definitive': the broader meaning (absence of probability arising from evidence) and the narrower meaning (absence of any probability). The passage intends the broader meaning. The discussion then applies this principle to the Quranic verse 'three quroo'' (Q 2:228). The author argues that since 'three' is a specific term, it must be fulfilled exactly, without excess or deficiency. The Shafi'i interpretation of 'qur'' as purity (tuhur) leads to either counting the purity in which divorce occurred, resulting in two purities and part of a third (less than three), or not counting it, resulting in three purities plus part of another (more than three). Either way, the specific number is not met precisely. The Hanafi interpretation of 'qur'' as menstruation (hayd) gives exactly three menstruations after the divorce (the purity in which divorce occurred is not a menstruation and is not counted; the three subsequent menstruations complete the count without excess or deficiency). The passage also addresses the potential objection that if a woman is divorced during menstruation (which is prohibited), the Hanafis do not count that menstruation, leading to a deficiency. Two answers are given: (1) The verse refers to legislated divorce (occurring in purity), so the objection does not apply; (2) If needed, part of a fourth menstruation can be added to complete the three, though a single menstruation is indivisible in the Shari'ah — this is a subtle point.

Key Terms
خاص (khaas): A term that is specific in its indication, referring to a particular meaning or individuals, as opposed to 'aam (general). Here, it is defined as a word that, when considered without external factors, necessitates its ruling definitively.
قطعي (qat'i): Definitive; absence of probability arising from evidence (the broader meaning used here).
قرء (qur'): A homonym used for both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tuhr). In this verse, the Hanafis adopt the meaning of menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument, why cannot 'qur'' mean purity in Quran 2:228?
A) Because purity is not a valid concept in Islamic law.
B) Because if it meant purity, the number three would either be less than three or more than three, violating the specific term 'three'.
C) Because the word 'qur'' is only used for menstruation in the Quran.
D) Because Imam Abu Hanifa explicitly stated it means menstruation without any reasoning.

Key Takeaway
A specific term in the Quran, like the number 'three', demands exact fulfillment; the Hanafi interpretation of 'qur'' as menstruation is derived from this principle of khas being qat'i.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Meaning of Quru' in the Verse of Iddah
Analyzing the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over 'quru'' in Surah al-Baqarah 2:228

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic ambiguity in the term 'quru'' and evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments regarding its meaning in Surah al-Baqarah 2:228.

Core Idea
The Hanafi argument that 'quru'' means menstruation hinges on the specific count of three, which purity periods cannot fulfill without contradiction.

The interpretation of a single word in the Quran can lead to a significant difference in legal rulings. In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over the meaning of 'quru'' in Surah al-Baqarah (2:228), which determines the waiting period (iddah) for a divorced woman. By analyzing the linguistic and legal arguments of both schools, you will understand how usul al-fiqh principles are applied to resolve textual ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested text and its ambiguity
Locate the Quranic verse (Surah al-Baqarah 2:228) and note that the term 'quru'' is a homonym, meaning either menstruation or purity periods. This ambiguity is the root of the dispute.
The verse is considered qati' al-thubut but zanni al-dalala due to the homonymy.
2
State each school's position
State clearly: Hanafi (Abu Hanifa) holds 'quru'' means menstruations; Shafi'i holds it means purity periods. This leads to different iddah lengths.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi iddah = three menstruations; Shafi'i iddah = three purity cycles (approx. three months).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof from the specific number
The Hanafis argue that the word 'three' (thalathah) is specific (khass). If 'quru'' meant purity, counting the purity in which divorce occurred leads to either two purities plus part of a third (if counted) or three purities plus part of a fourth (if not counted), both violating the exact count of three. Only menstruations satisfy the exact count without excess or deficiency.
This argument uses the usul principle that khass is binding (wujub al-'amal bi al-khass).
4
Understand the Hanafi rejoinder regarding split menstruation
A Shafi'i objection: if a woman is divorced during menstruation, Hanafis do not count that menstruation, so three full menstruations plus part of a fourth would be required, again violating the specific count. The Hanafi response: the verse addresses prescribed divorce (in a state of purity), and the Shari'ah does not split a single menstruation, as evidenced by the slave woman's iddah (two full menstruations instead of one and a half). Thus, a full fourth menstruation becomes obligatory.
This response relies on the principle that a single menstruation is indivisible for iddah purposes.
5
Evaluate the author's unique argument against the Shafi'i position
The author presents a decisive argument: if part of a purity is considered a full purity (as Shafi'is might argue to complete three purities), then a small part of the third purity would end the iddah, allowing immediate remarriage. This contradicts the consensus that the iddah must be complete. Therefore, the Shafi'i interpretation is untenable.
The author claims this argument is unique to his commentary.

متنازعہ میں بھی حیض رابع کامل واجب ہو جائیگا اور اس قسم کا جواب سوافع حضرات نہیں دے سکتے کیونکہ رابع کا نہ بعض اور نہ کل کچھ بھی واجب نہیں کرتے بلکہ بعض حصہ طہر اور اس کے علاوہ دو طہر سے عدت کو ختم کر دیتے ہیں ۔ على ان بعض الطهر یہ ایک سوال مقدر کا جواب ہے دھوان يقال خلاصہ سوال یہ ہے کہ آپ یہ کیوں کہتے ہیں انه اذا احتسب کہ جب طہر اول جس میں طلاق واقع ہوئی ہے اس کو عدت میں شمار کر لیا جائے تو پھر واجب دو طہر اور بعض حصہ طہر کا ہوا تین مکمل نہ ہوئے بلکہ اس صورت میں واجب تین ہوئے لان بعض الظهر ظہر یہ تین ہونے کی دلیل ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ بعض حصہ طہر کا بھی طہر کہلاتا ہے کیونکہ طہر ادنی ما یطلق علیہ الظہر کو کہا جاتا یعنی اونی مقدار جس پر طہر کا اطلاق ہو سکتا ہے مثلا ایک ساعتہ طہر پر طہر کا اطلاق ہو سکتا ہے لہذا اس کو طہر کہا جائے گا وہ بعض حصہ بھی طہر ہو گا لہذا تین طہر ہو گئے عدت مکمل ہو جائے گی فنقول یہ جواب ہے اس اشکال مذکور کا حاصل یہ کہ بعض طہر طہر نہیں ہو سکتا - لانه لو كان كذلك اگر ائے ہو تو پھر اول طہر اور ثالث میں فرق نہیں ہوگا فسلفی فی الثالث پھر تیسرے طہر کے بعض حصہ و ظہر قرار دیا جائے کیونکہ ادنی ما یطلق علیہ الظہر کے لحاظ سے یہ بھی طہر ہے فینبغی انه اذا معنی پھر تو لائق ہے کہ جب طہر ثالث سے کچھ تھوڑا حصہ گزر جائے تو آپ کے حسب قول تین طہر ہو چکے ہیں تو اس عورت کا طہر ثالث کے قلیل مقدار گزر جانے کے ساتھ اس عورت کا دوسرے خاوند کے ساتھ نکاح صحیح ہو جانا چاہتے اور اس عورت کا تزوج حلال ہو۔ وهذا خلاف الاجماع حالانکہ یہ خلاف اجماع ہے ۔ شوافع بھی اس نکاح کو ناجائز قرار دیتے میں هذا الجواب قاطع تو یہ جواب شوافع کے شبہہ کو بالکل قطع کر دیتا ہے وقد تضررت بذا میں اس جواب کے ساتھ متفرد ہوں قوم نے ایک اور جواب بھی دیا تھا وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر طہر مجموع ما تخلل بین الدین کا نام ہے تو پھر آپ کا اشکال وارد ہی نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ میں طہر میں طلاق ہوئی ہے وہ مجموع ما نتخلل سے کم مقدار ہے لہذا اس صورت میں عدت دو طہر اور بعض حصہ طہر کا ہوا اگر طہر مجموع ما يتخلل بین الدین کا نام نہیں بلکہ بعض مقدار کو بھی طہر کہا جاتا ہے تو پھر عدت صرف ایک طہر یا اس سے بھی کم مقدار کے ساتھ عدت مکمل ہو
And some of the purity period becomes obligatory in the iddah, still the requirement of three is not fulfilled. In the first case, less than three; in the second case, more than three became necessary, contrary to if menstruation is meant: then the purity period in which divorce occurred, after it, three menstruations occur exactly without any increase or decrease. Know that the word 'quru'' is a homonym, i.e., it is used for both menstruation and purity. In the verse of Allah: 'And divorced women shall wait by themselves for three quru',' according to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'quru'' means menstruation, and according to Imam Shafi'i, it means purity. We (Hanafis) say: if 'quru'' means purity, then the specific requirement becomes invalid because the word 'three' is specific, and prescribed divorce is that which occurs in a state of purity, not in menstruation. So the purity in which divorce occurred: if it is not counted in the iddah but three purities besides it are considered, then three purities and part of a purity become obligatory, exceeding the number three. And if it is counted, as Imam Shafi'i's school holds, then two purities and part of a purity result, and the amount of three is not fulfilled. Since the word 'three' is specific and indicates a decisive ruling, it can only be satisfied if menstruation is meant. Therefore, 'quru'' must mean menstruation. That is the Hanafi school. However, a similar problem arises for Hanafis when a woman is divorced during menstruation: they do not count that menstruation in the iddah, but consider three besides it, resulting in three menstruations and part of a menstruation, exceeding the required three. Thus, even on the Hanafi school, the specific requirement is not fulfilled in that case. The answer: first, the discourse is about prescribed divorce, because the Shari'ah's primary concern is to present the rulings of prescribed acts; the rulings of non-prescribed acts are derived from consensus and other sources. Second, the requirement of three must be fulfilled, so for that purpose some days of a fourth menstruation are added to compensate for the deficiency of the first menstruation in which divorce occurred. However, the Shari'ah does not allow splitting a single menstruation. Observe that the iddah of a slave woman, which is half of the free woman's iddah, would require one and a half menstruations, but the Shari'ah did not split it; rather, it prescribed two complete menstruations. Therefore, in the disputed case, a full fourth menstruation becomes obligatory. The Shafi'is cannot give this answer because they do not make any part or all of a fourth menstruation obligatory; rather, they complete the iddah with part of a purity and two purities besides it. As for 'some of the purity', this is an answer to an implied question. The gist of the question: Why do you say that if the first purity in which divorce occurred is counted, then two purities and part of a purity become obligatory, not completing three? But in that case, three become obligatory because part of a purity is also called purity, since the least amount to which the term 'purity' applies is called purity, e.g., one hour of purity can be called purity, so that part is still purity, hence three purities are complete. We say: the answer to this objection is that part of a purity cannot be purity. Because if it were so, then there would be no difference between the first and third purity. Then in the third purity, a part would be considered purity, since the least amount to which 'purity' applies is also purity. Therefore, when a small part of the third purity passes, according to your statement, three purities would be complete, and the woman could validly marry another husband after that small amount of the third purity, making her marriage permissible. This is against consensus. This answer decisively refutes the Shafi'i doubt. This answer is unique to me. The people (other Hanafis) also gave another answer: if 'purity' means the entire interval between two menstruations, then your objection does not arise, because the purity in which divorce occurred is less than the entire interval, so the iddah becomes two purities and part of a purity. But if 'purity' does not mean the entire interval, and even a small amount is called purity, then the iddah could be completed with just one purity or even less.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the interpretation of the word 'quru'' (أقراء) in the Qur'anic verse on the waiting period (iddah) of divorced women. The key issue is whether 'quru'' means menstruations or periods of purity, leading to different rules for the length of iddah. The author first explains that 'quru'' is a homonym. Imam Abu Hanifa holds it means menstruations, while Imam Shafi'i holds it means purity periods. The Hanafis argue that interpreting it as purity leads to a contradiction with the word 'three' being specific. The passage then addresses a counter-objection: if a woman is divorced during menstruation, the Hanafis do not count that menstruation, so the number of menstruations exceeds three. The answer distinguishes between prescribed and prohibited divorce, and explains that the Shari'ah does not split a single menstruation, citing the example of a slave woman's iddah being two full menstruations instead of one and a half. The passage concludes with a powerful argument against the Shafi'i position: if part of a purity is considered a full purity, then the iddah could end with just a small part of the third purity, which would allow immediate remarriage—something that is against consensus. This final argument is claimed to be unique to the author.

Key Terms
قرء (quru'): A homonym meaning either menstruation or purity period.
ثلاثة قروء (thalathatu quru'): Three quru' — the waiting period for a divorced free woman.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute over the meaning of 'quru''?
A) The permissibility of remarriage after divorce
B) The length of the iddah: three menstruations (Hanafi) vs. three purity periods (Shafi'i)
C) The validity of divorce during menstruation
D) The number of witnesses required for divorce

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'quru'' in the iddah verse means menstruations, supported by the specific number three and the indivisibility of a single menstruation, which the purity interpretation cannot satisfy without contradiction.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
