The Tawaqquf Position on General Terms
Presentation of the tawaqquf position on general terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the tawaqquf position regarding general terms and identify its three main arguments.

Core Idea
Tawaqquf holds that general terms require evidence to clarify their scope due to the plurality of the plural.

In this passage, we encounter the view called tawaqquf: suspending judgment on the scope of a general term until evidence clarifies. The author presents three rational arguments: the variability of plural numbers, the need for emphasis, and the shared nature of plural between singular and plural. Understanding this position helps contrast with the Hanafi view that general terms are definite until specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of tawaqquf
State that tawaqquf means suspending judgment on the scope of a general term until evidence clarifies.
The text says: 'the ruling of the general term is suspension... until evidence comes.'
2
Examine the first argument: number variability
Note that the plural form has no fixed number; plural of few (3-10) and plural of many (10+). Without specification, the intended number is unknown.
Example: 'for Zayd, upon him dirhams' could be 3 to 10 or more.
3
Examine the second argument: need for emphasis
General terms are often emphasized with 'kull' or 'ajma‘', which would be unnecessary if they already covered all individuals.
This suggests the term itself does not automatically include all.
4
Examine the third argument: shared usage
The plural form is shared (mushtarak) between singular and plural, as in the Quranic verse where 'al-nās' first means a single person and then many.
Quran 3:173: 'those to whom the people said: indeed the people have gathered against you.'
5
Consider the practical implication
Thus, according to this view, one cannot make a definitive ruling from a general term without additional evidence.
This contrasts with the Hanafi view that general terms are qat‘i until takhsis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل حكم العام التوقف عند البعض الخ بعض حضرات کے نزدیک عام کا حکم توقف ہے یعنی جمیع افراد یا بعض افراد کو حکم قطعا شامل ہے یا کیسے اس معاملہ میں توقف کیا جائے حتى يقوم الدليل لانه مجمل کیونکہ عام محمل ہے لاختلاف عدد المجمع کیونکہ جمع جو صیغہ عموم کا ہے اس کے اعداد مختلف ہیں کوئی متعین عدد نہیں ہے جمع قلت میں تین سے دس تک بر عدد مراد ہو سکتا ہے اور جمع کثرۃ میں دس سے الی مالا نہایت بر عدد مراد ہو سکتا ہے۔ نانه قال الي مثلا جب کہتا ہے لزید علی افلس تو اس کا بیان تین سے دس تک صحیح ہے یہ مجمل ہوا جب تک کوئی دلیل تعیین قائم نہ ہو تو مراد معلوم نہیں ہو سکتی دانه يؤكد یہ دوسری دلیل ہے مجمل ہونے کی خلاصہ یہ کہ اس عام کی تاکید لفظ کل اور اجمع سے کی جاتی ہے اگر مستغرق لجميع الافراد ہوتا تو اس تاکید کی ضرورت پیش نہ آئی ولانه يذكر الم یعنی توقف والوں کی یہ اور دلیل ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ لفظ عام مشترک بین المعانی معلوم ہوتا ہے جیسے لفظ جمع ذکر کر کے مراد واحد ہوتا ہے اور کبھی اس لفظ جمع سے مراد کثیر افراد ہوتے نہیں جیسے اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول الذين قال لهم الناس ان الناس قد جمعوا لکم تو پہلے الناس سے مراد نیم بن مسعود یا کوئی اور ایک اعرابی مراد ہے اور الناس ثانی سے مراد اہل مکہ میں تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ یہ صیغہ مشترک بین الواحد والکثیر ہے تو جب تک قرینہ اور دلیل کی بنا پر کسی ایک منی
Chapter: The ruling of the general term is suspension according to some. Some scholars hold that the ruling of the general term is suspension, i.e., whether it includes all individuals or some individuals definitively, or how? In this matter, one should suspend judgment until evidence comes, because it is ambiguous (mujmal), because the general term is ambiguous due to the difference in numbers of the plural. Because the plural, which is the form of generality, has varying numbers; no specific number is fixed. In the plural of few, three to ten can be intended; in the plural of many, ten to infinity can be intended. For example, when one says 'for Zayd, upon him dirhams' [as a debt], it is valid to interpret it as three to ten; it is ambiguous until an evidence specifying it is established, so the intended meaning cannot be known. [Another reason:] It is emphasized. That is the second evidence for it being ambiguous: this general term is emphasized by words like 'kull' and 'ajma‘'; if it were all-encompassing of all individuals, such emphasis would not be needed. And because it is shared... i.e., the proponents of suspension have another evidence: the general term is apparently shared between meanings, like when the plural form is mentioned but singular is intended, and sometimes the plural form intends many individuals, like in the saying of Allah: 'those to whom the people said: indeed the people have gathered against you.' The first 'people' refers to Nu'aym ibn Mas'ud or some other Bedouin, and the second 'people' refers to the people of Makkah. So it is known that this form is shared between singular and plural. So until [clarification] by context and evidence...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the view of some scholars (apparently a group among the Usuliyyun) that general terms (al-āmm) require suspension (tawaqquf) — i.e., one cannot definitively rule on whether all or some individuals are included until evidence clarifies. The author summarizes three arguments: (1) The plural form is ambiguous because the number is not fixed (between 3-10 for plural of few, and 10+ for plural of many). (2) General terms are often emphatically specified with words like 'kull' or 'ajma‘', which would be unnecessary if they were already all-encompassing. (3) The plural form is shared (mushtarak) between singular and plural meanings, as seen in the Quranic verse where 'al-nās' first means a single person and later means many. Therefore, one must wait for a contextual clue or evidence. The passage does not give the opposing view here.

Key Terms
عام (‘āmm): A general term that encompasses all members of its category.
توقف (tawaqquf): Suspension of judgment until evidence clarifies the intended scope of a general term.
مجمل (mujmal): An ambiguous term that requires clarification.
جمع قلت (jam‘ qillah): Plural of small number, from 3 to 10.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the view of tawaqquf regarding general terms (al-‘āmm)?
A) General terms are absolute and require no clarification.
B) Judgment must be suspended until evidence clarifies the intended scope.
C) General terms always include all individuals.
D) General terms are always ambiguous and never clear.

Key Takeaway
Tawaqquf is a minority position that holds general terms are ambiguous until evidence clarifies, based on the plurality of the plural, the need for emphasis, and shared usage.

General Terms ('Amm) and the Hanafi Refutation of Tawaqquf
Refuting the view that general terms require suspension and affirming the Hanafi position that they obligate ruling on all individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that 'amm is decisive (qat'i) and refute the argument that general terms require suspension (tawaqquf).

Core Idea
General terms in Shari'ah obligate ruling on all individuals immediately; tawaqquf is rejected.

This passage presents the arguments of those who claim that general terms ('amm) are ambiguous and require suspension until evidence clarifies their scope. The Hanafis respond with reasoned refutations, establishing that 'amm is linguistically clear and legally decisive. You will learn how the Hanafi school uses both rational proof and the practice of the Companions to defend this fundamental usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that the passage first presents the three arguments of those who advocate tawaqquf: (1) varying plural numbers cause ambiguity, (2) general terms need emphasis, (3) plurals are used metaphorically for single individuals.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
For each argument, note the Hanafi counter: (1) all numbers are intended unless specified, (2) emphasis confirms generality, (3) metaphorical use does not negate real meaning. Understand the logical reasoning behind each response.
This step shows how Hanafi usul defends the immediate applicability of 'amm.
3
Examine the minimum-number view
The passage presents a variant view that 'amm indicates only the minimum number (e.g., three in plurals). Note the example of 'دراهم' and the Hanafi refutation using rational context.
The minimum is taken only when generality is rationally impossible, not as a default.
4
Extract the positive Hanafi position
Identify the core claim: 'عندنا وعند الشافعي أن العام يوجب الحكم في الكل'. Understand that this is the mufta bihi (operative) position, supported by rational and consensual proof.
Generality is an intended meaning in discourse, so there must be linguistic forms for it.
5
Reflect on the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical difference: if a general command is given, Hanafis apply it to all instances immediately; the tawaqquf view would suspend until specification. This impacts how quickly rulings are derived.
This difference is critical in deriving fiqh rulings from primary sources.

ہے لاختلاف عدد المجمع کیونکہ جمع جو صیغہ عموم کا ہے اس کے اعداد مختلف ہیں کوئی متعین عدد نہیں ہے جمع قلت میں تین سے دس تک بر عدد مراد ہو سکتا ہے اور جمع کثرۃ میں دس سے الی مالا نہایت بر عدد مراد ہو المعانی معلوم ہوتا ہے جیسے لفظ جمع ذکر کر کے مراد واحد نہوتا ہے اور کبھی اس لفظ جمع سے مراد کثیر افراد ہوتے نہیں جیسے اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول الذين قال لهم الناس ان الناس قد جمعوا لکم تو پہلے الناس سے مراد نیم بن مسعود یا کوئی اور ایک اعرابی مراد ہے اور الناس ثانی سے مراد اہل مکہ میں تو اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ یہ صیغہ مشترک بین الواحد والکثیر ہے تو جب تک قرینہ اور دلیل کی بنا پر کسی ایک منی کی تعیین یا ترجیح نہ ہو جائے کوئی معنی نہیں لیا جا سکتا لہذا توقف ضروری ہوا ۔ اول کا جواب یہ دیا جاتا ہے کہ کل عدد مراد ہوگا کیونکہ درمیانی اعداد میں سے بعض کو لینا اور مریض کو نہ لینا ترجیح بلا مرجح ہے ثانی کا جواب یہ ہے کہ تاکید دلیل عموم و استغراق ہے کیونکہ اگر صیغہ جمع میں عموم و استغراق نہ ہوتا تو پھر لفظ کل اور اجمعون تاکید نہ ہو سکتا بلکہ تاسیس ہو جاتی ثالث کا جواب یہ ہے کہ واحد پر اطلاق جمع از قبیلہ مجاز ہے اور اطلاق علی الکثیر حقیقت ہے اشتراک نہیں ہے کیونکہ مجاز اولى من الاشتراک ہوتا ہے ۔ اور جمع سے مراد عام ہے جو صیغہ جمع ہو جیسے الرجال یا اسم جمع ہو جیسے الناس کو شامل ہے ۔ وعند البعض يثبت الادنی یعنی بعض کے نزدک صیغہ عام میں ادنی اور اقل عدد مراد ہوگا ۔ صیغہ جمع میں تین اور اسم جنس وغیرہ میں صرف واحد مراد ہوگا لانہ المسیقی کیونکہ یہی ادنی مقدار کا مراد ہونا امر متیقن اور یقینی ہے اس سے ماوراء مشکوک ہے اس لئے یہی مقدار متیقن مراد ہوگی - خانه اذا قال اونی مقدار والوں کی دلیل ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ جب کوئی شخص کہتا ہے کہ لفلان علی دراهم تو میاں بالاتفاق تین واجب ہوتے ہیں جو لفظ در اہم کی ادنی مقدار ہے ۔ لکنا نقول لیکن ہم اس کے جواب میں کہتے ہیں کہ یہاں عموم چونکہ مراد نہیں ہو سکتا کہ کائنات دعالم کے جمیع در اہم کا کسی کے حق میں اپنے اوپر اقرار کرنا عقلا محال ہے اس لئے اخص الخصوص مراد ہوگا یہ تو قرینہ عقلیہ کے ساتھ مراد ہوئی اس کا حقیقی مفہوم نہیں ہے اور کلام حقیقی مفہوم میں تھی وعندنا وعند الشافعي الا احناف و شوافع کے نزدیک عام يوجب الحکم فی الکل حکم کو کل افراد عام میں واجب کر دے گا جیسے جانی القوم یہ لفظ قوم جو عام ہے یہ موجب نسبت حکم محبتی کا لیکل فرد من افراد القوم ہے لان العموم یہ مذہب مختار پر دلیل عقلی ہے تخلاصہ یہ کہ عموم ایک ایسا معنی ہے جو مقصود للناس ہے تو ضروری ہے کہ کوئی ایسا لفظ ہو جو اس معنی مقصود پر دال بالوضع نہو خان المعانی یعنی جو معانی مقصود في التخاطب و المحاورات ہوتے ہیں عادہ ان کے لئے الفاظ کی وضع ضرور ہوتی ہے لہذا جب عموم ایک مقصودی معنی ہے تو اس کے لئے بھی وضع لفظ ضروری ہو گی ۔ وقد قال علی فی الجمع التم گویا یہ ایک دلیل اجاعی علی المذہب المختار ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے صحابہ اور غیر صحابہ سے احتجاج بالعموم ثابت ہے جیسے حضرت علیؓ نے فرمایا کہ دو بہنوں کے درمیان بملک یمین وطیا جمع کرنے کو آیت
The chapter: The ruling of the general (العام): tawaqquf (suspension) according to some. According to some, the ruling of the general is tawaqquf, i.e., whether the ruling definitely includes all individuals or some is suspended until evidence establishes it. This is because it is mujmal (ambiguous). Because the general is mujmal due to the difference in the numbers of the plural. Since the plural, which is the form of generality, has different numbers; no specific number is fixed. In the plural of few, three to ten can be intended, and in the plural of many, ten to infinity can be intended. For example, when one says 'لزيد علي أفلس' (for Zaid is a debt of dirhams), the statement can be clarified from three to ten; it is mujmal until a clarifying evidence is established, so the intended meaning is unknown. Second proof: it is emphasized (يؤكد). Summary: that this general is emphasized with the words 'كل' and 'أجمع'. If it were inclusive of all individuals, this emphasis would not be needed. Third proof: they mention the issue of commonality between meanings. Summary: that the general term appears to be common between meanings, like the plural word sometimes used to mean one, e.g., Allah's saying: 'الذين قال لهم الناس إن الناس قد جمعوا لكم' – here the first 'الناس' means one person (Nu'aym ibn Mas'ud or a Bedouin), and the second 'الناس' means the people of Mecca. So this shows that the form is common between one and many. Therefore, until a context or evidence specifies or gives preference to one, no meaning can be taken; thus tawaqquf is necessary. Response to the first: it is said that all numbers are intended, because taking some of the intermediate numbers and not taking the rest is a preference without a preponderant. Response to the second: that emphasis is evidence of generality and inclusiveness, because if the plural form did not have generality and inclusiveness, then 'كل' and 'أجمعون' could not be emphases but would be foundations (تأسيس). Response to the third: that applying the plural to a single individual is a metaphorical usage, and applying it to many is the real meaning, not commonality, because metaphor is preferable to commonality (مجاز أولى من الاشتراك). And the plural refers to general, whether the plural form like 'الرجال' or the collective noun like 'الناس'. And according to some, the minimum (الأدنى) is intended. In plural forms, three is intended, and in generic nouns, only one is intended, because this minimum is certain and what is beyond is doubtful, so only this certain quantity is intended. Their proof: when one says 'لفلان على دراهم', three are agreed to be obligatory, which is the minimum of the word dirhams. But we say in response: here generality cannot be intended because it is rationally impossible for one to acknowledge all dirhams of the whole world over himself, so the most specific meaning (أخص الخصوص) is intended with a rational context, and it is not the real meaning, and speech is in the real meaning. And according to us and according to al-Shafi'i [the general obligates the ruling in all], the Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that the general obligates the ruling in all, i.e., it makes the ruling obligatory for every individual of the general, like 'جاني القوم' – the word 'قوم' which is general obligates the ruling of love for every individual of the people. This is because generality is a meaning intended by people, so it is necessary that there be a word that indicates this intended meaning by convention. Since generality is an intended meaning, there must be a word placed for it. And 'Ali said about the plural... as if this is a consensus evidence for the chosen madhhab, the summary of which is: that the companions and others used generality as evidence (الاحتجاج بالعموم) is established. For example, 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said that between two sisters, by ownership of the right hand, marrying them together is [prohibited] by the verse
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion about the nature of general terms ('amm). It presents the arguments of those who advocate tawaqquf (suspension) before accepting the generality of a term, and then provides Hanafi responses. The advocates of tawaqquf argue that general terms are mujmal (ambiguous) because the plural form can indicate different numbers, because general terms are often emphasized (suggesting they may not be fully inclusive on their own), and because sometimes the plural is used for a single individual (as in the Quranic verse about 'الناس'). The Hanafis respond: (1) all numbers are intended unless specified, (2) emphasis actually confirms generality, and (3) using a plural for one is metaphorical; the real usage is for many. The passage then mentions a subgroup who hold that a general term indicates only the minimum number (e.g., three in plural, one in generic nouns). This view is refuted by saying that in the example of 'دراهم', the minimum is taken because a rational context prevents generality. Finally, the Hanafi-Shafi'i position is affirmed: general terms obligate the ruling on all individuals immediately, without suspension. This is supported by intellectual proof (generality is a common communicative intention) and by consensus (companions used general terms as evidence, e.g., 'Ali's statement about two sisters). Although the passage cuts off at the end, it clearly advocates the Hanafi position that 'amm is decisive (qat'i) and does not require suspension.

Key Terms
عام ('amm): A word that indicates all individuals of a given category by virtue of its linguistic structure.
توقف (tawaqquf): Suspension of assigning a meaning to a general word until evidence clarifies its intended scope.
مجمل (mujmal): A word that is ambiguous in its meaning and requires clarification.
جمع قلت (jam' qilla): Plural of few: forms used for three to ten objects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position regarding general terms ('amm)?
A) They require suspension (tawaqquf) until evidence specifies
B) They are ambiguous (mujmal) and need clarification
C) They obligate the ruling on all individuals immediately
D) They indicate only the minimum number

Key Takeaway
General terms in Shari'ah are decisive (qat'i) and obligate the ruling on all individuals immediately; suspension (tawaqquf) is rejected by Hanafis.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Amm Indicates All Individuals: Hanafi Rational Proof
Defending the Hanafi view that 'amm is huijah over all individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on amm as huijah for all individuals and evaluate the rational proof and Companion evidence presented.

Core Idea
Amm obligates the ruling on every individual; generality is an intended meaning with a dedicated word.

This passage presents a robust Hanafi defense of the principle that a general term (amm) applies to all its individuals unless there is a clear indication to restrict it. It refutes three objections about the plural form, discusses the minimum number interpretation, and provides both rational proof and practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Refute the objections
Analyze the three objections against the plural form indicating generality: (1) no preference among numbers, (2) emphasis words like kull confirm generality, (3) applying plural to one is metaphorical, while to many is literal.
Metaphor takes precedence over homonymy.
2
Examine the minimum number claim
Consider the view that plural indicates a minimum of three and generic nouns indicate one, using the example of acknowledging dirhams.
The Hanafi response: the instance is due to a rational indication (qarinah aqliyyah), not the real meaning.
3
Understand the rational proof
Follow the argument: generality is an intended meaning in discourse, therefore there must be a word conventionally placed for it, just like other intended meanings.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
4
Apply to the Companion examples
See how Ali used the generality of 'ma malakat aymanukum' to argue that all possessed women are included, and how he resolved the conflict with the prohibition of combining sisters by giving preponderance to the prohibitor. Also see the disagreement between Ali and Ibn Mas'ud on the 'iddah of a pregnant widow, where both used amm as huijah.
This conflict shows that amm is huijah even when outcomes differ.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
Conclude that amm obligates the ruling on every individual unless there is a specific takhsis; this is the preferred (mukhtar) position supported by reason and practice.

شامل ہو اور ان تجمعوا بين الاختين بھی ہر قسم کی جمع کو شامل نہو تو معلوم ہوا کہ عام سر فرد پر موجب حکم ہوتا ہے و ابن مسعود جعل قوله تعالى واولات الاحمال الج یعنی ابن مسعود نے اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول و ان یضمن حملین اپنے عموم کے ساتھ مقتضی ہے کہ حاملہ عورت خواہ مطلقہ ہو یا متوفی عنہا زوجہا ہو تو اس کی عدت وضع حمل ہے تو ان دونوں آیات میں بحق عورت حاملہ متوفی عنہا زر و جہا میں تعارض ہے اور یہ تعارض اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جب دونوں آیات بلحاظ عموم نہر فرد کو شامل ہوں تو پہلی آیت بھی حاملہ کو شامل ہوگی اور دوسری آیت بھی متوفی عنہا کو شامل ہوگی تو تعارض ہو گا اور اس تعارض کو دفع کرنے کے لئے حضرت علی نے ان دونوں آیات ہیں یہ موافقت کی کہ حاملہ متوفی عنہا زوجها ابعد الاجلین کے ساتھ عدت گزارے یعنی اگر چار ماہ دس دن مدت ابعد و اطول بنتی ہو تو میہی عدت ہوگی اگر وضع حمل کی مدت البعد واطول ہو تو پھر یہی عدت ہوگی اور حضرت ابن مسعود نے طریق نسخ کو اختیار کیا اس عورت کے متعلق جس کو دونوں آیات شامل ہیں یعنی حاملہ متوفی عنہا زوجہا تو انھوں نے فرمایا کہ سورۃ بقرہ پہلے نازل ہوئی اور نساء قصری یعنی طلاق بعد میں لہذا یہی اس کیلئے
If there is no determination or preference, no meaning can be taken, therefore pause is necessary. The answer to the first is: the whole number is intended, because taking some of the intermediate numbers and not taking the intended is preference without preponderance. The answer to the second is: emphasis is evidence of generality and inclusiveness, because if the plural form did not have generality and inclusiveness, then the words 'kull' and 'ajma'un' would not be emphasis but would be founding. The answer to the third is: applying a plural to a single is metaphorical, and applying it to many is literal, not common, because metaphor takes precedence over common. And 'jama' refers to the general, whether it is a plural form like 'al-rijal' or a collective noun like 'al-nas'. And according to some, the minimum is established: according to some, the general form intends the minimum and least number; in the plural form three, and in the generic noun etc. only one is intended, because this is the certain and sure intended matter, and beyond that is doubtful, so this certain amount is intended. Example: when one says 'to so-and-so upon me dirhams', then by agreement three become obligatory, which is the minimum of the word 'dirham'. But we say in answer: here, because generality cannot be intended - that to acknowledge all the dirhams of the universe as owed is rationally impossible - therefore the most specific of the specific is intended; this is due to a rational indication, not its real meaning, and speech is in its real meaning. And according to us and according to al-Shafi'i [except?] - the Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that the general obligates the ruling upon all, like 'kunta al-qaum' - the word 'qaum' which is general makes the ruling of 'jani' obligatory upon every individual of the people, because generality is, according to the preferred school, a rational proof. Summary: Generality is a meaning that is intended for people, so it is necessary that there be a word that indicates this intended meaning by convention. Because meanings that are intended in discourse and conversation are customarily assigned words; so when generality is an intended meaning, the assignment of a word for it is also necessary. And 'Ali said regarding the combination [of sisters] ... this is a consensus proof upon the preferred school, the summary of which is: from the Companions and others, arguing by generality is established, as 'Ali said that the verse 'ma malakat aymanukum' makes lawful sexual intercourse between two sisters by right hand possession, because in this verse 'ma malakat aymanukum' is a general word indicating that every possessed woman's intercourse is lawful, whether she is with her sister or alone. And the other verse prohibits this type of combination of sisters, i.e., 'an tajma'u bayna al-ukhtayn', because this verse indicates that the combination of sisters, whether by marriage or by intercourse via right hand possession, all of that is forbidden. So the prohibitor is preponderant, meaning when permissibility and prohibition conflict, the prohibition is preponderant, as will appear in the chapter on conflict: that the prohibitor is preponderant over the permissible. So this conflict can occur only when 'ma malakat' includes the possessed woman, and 'an tajma'u bayna al-ukhtayn' also includes every type of combination, so it is known that the general makes the ruling upon the individual. And Ibn Mas'ud made the saying of Allah ta'ala 'wa ulatu al-ahmal' as abrogating for the continuation from you, such that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband dies is giving birth. 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud differed regarding the pregnant [widow]. The basic issue is that the verse of Surah al-Baqarah 'wa alladhina yutawaffawna minkum...' by its generality requires that the woman whose husband has died, whether pregnant or not, has an 'iddah of four months and ten days, and the verse of Surah al-Nisa' al-Qusra i.e. Surah al-Talaq 'wa ulatu al-ahmal ajaluhunna an yada'na hamlahunna' by its generality requires that a pregnant woman, whether divorced or a widow, has the 'iddah of giving birth. So between these two verses regarding the pregnant widow there is conflict, and this conflict can occur only when both verses include the individual by virtue of generality; so the first verse also includes the pregnant woman, and the second verse also includes the widow, so there is conflict. And to remove this conflict, 'Ali reconciled them: that the pregnant widow should observe the 'iddah with the longer of the two terms: if the period of four months and ten days is longer and greater, then that is the 'iddah; if the period of giving birth is longer and greater, then that is the 'iddah. And Ibn Mas'ud adopted the method of abrogation: regarding the woman whom both verses include - the pregnant widow - he said that Surah al-Baqarah was revealed first, and al-Nisa' al-Qusra i.e. al-Talaq later, therefore this is for her...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion defending the Hanafi position on the general term ('amm). It begins by refuting three objections to the claim that the plural form indicates generality: first, if no specific number is preferred, the whole number must be intended to avoid arbitrary preference; second, the existence of emphatic words like 'kull' and 'ajma'un proves that the plural itself carries generality, otherwise these would be tasis (foundation) not ta'kid (emphasis); third, applying a plural to a single individual is metaphorical, while applying it to many is literal, and metaphor takes precedence over homonymy. The passage then notes that some scholars hold that the minimum number is intended: three for plural forms and one for generic nouns, citing the example of acknowledging dirhams where three become obligatory by agreement. The Hanafi response is that this instance is governed by a rational indication (qarinah 'aqliyyah) that restricts the general, not by the real meaning. The author then states the Hanafi and Shafi'i view that 'amm obligates the ruling on all individuals, supported by a rational proof: since generality is an intended meaning in discourse, there must be a word placed for it by convention. Two proofs from the Companions follow: first, 'Ali's argument that the general term 'ma malakat aymanukum' includes all possessed women, and its conflict with the prohibition of combining sisters is resolved by giving preponderance to the prohibitor, demonstrating that 'amm is huijah. Second, the ikhtilaf between 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud regarding the 'iddah of a pregnant widow is recounted: 'Ali reconciled the two verses by taking the longer period, while Ibn Mas'ud abrogated the earlier verse (Surah al-Baqarah) by the later (Surah al-Talaq). The passage concludes that this disagreement itself shows that both Companions considered 'amm to be huijah for their arguments.

Key Terms
عام (amm): A word that indicates an unrestricted universal, obligating the ruling on every individual that falls under it
صیغہ جمع (sighah jam'): The plural form (like 'al-rijal') that indicates generality by itself
تاکید (ta'kid): Emphasis, such as the words 'kull' and 'ajma'un', which confirm the generality of the plural

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view stated in this passage, what does the general term (amm) obligate?
A) Only the minimum number of individuals
B) The ruling on every individual included in the general
C) Only three individuals in case of plural forms
D) Nothing, it is ambiguous until clarified

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a general term (amm) obligates the ruling on every individual without exception, unless a specific indication (takhsis) is proven, based on rational proof and Companion practice.

Combining Two Sisters: Prohibition Overrides Permission
Analysis of conflict between two general Quranic verses and application of Hanafi rule that prohibitory text prevails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a prohibitory general text (muharram) takes precedence over a permissive general text (mubah) when they conflict, using the example of combining sisters.

Core Idea
When two general texts conflict, the prohibitor prevails over the permissive.

This passage analyzes a conflict between two Quranic verses: one generally permitting intercourse with slave women, and another prohibiting combining two sisters. The commentator demonstrates the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibitory text is given preponderance (tarjih) over a permissive text. We will see how this principle is applied to derive the ruling that combining sisters through ownership or marriage is unlawful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting general texts
Locate the permissive verse (ma malakat aymanukum) which broadly permits intercourse with owned women, and the prohibitory verse (an tajma'u bayn al-ukhtayn) which forbids combining two sisters.
Both are general (amm) in scope.
2
Determine the scope of each general text
The permissive applies to all owned women, including sisters. The prohibitory applies to any form of combining sisters, whether through marriage or ownership.
The prohibitory text explicitly covers 'bayn al-ukhtayn' without restriction.
3
Recognize the conflict
The permissive text would allow owning and having intercourse with two sisters simultaneously, but the prohibitory text forbids it. Thus, both cannot be applied together to this case.
Conflict arises only when both texts cover the same individual case.
4
Apply the Hanafi tarjih rule
According to Hanafi usul, when two general texts conflict, the prohibitory text (muharram) is given preponderance over the permissive text (mubah). This is a fixed rule of tarjih.
The rule is stated: 'al-muharram raajih 'ala al-mubah'.
5
Derive the final ruling
Since the prohibitory text prevails, combining two sisters is prohibited (haram) in all forms, overriding the general permission.
The ruling applies to both marriage and concubinage (milk al-yamin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ملکت ایمانکم حلال قرار دیتی ہے کیونکہ اس آیت میں ما ملکت ایمانکم عام لفظ ہے جو ہر مملوکہ کی وطی حلال ہونے پر دلالت کرتا ہے خواہ مجتمعہ مع الاخت ہو یا منفردہ ہو۔ ہو حرمتهما ايہ اور دوسری آیت اس قسم کی جمع بین الاختین کو حرام قرار دیتی ہے وھی ان تجمعوا بین الاختین کیونکہ یہ آیت دلالت کرتی ہے جمع بین الاختین خواہ نکا حا ہو یا وطیا بسملک الیمین ہو یہ سب حرام ہے فالمحرم راجح یعنی جب اباحۃ اور حرمت میں تعارض ہو تو محرم راجح ہوتا ہے جیسے فصل تعارض میں آئیگا کہ ان المحرم راج علی المیے تو یہ تعارض اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جب ما ملکت بر مملوکہ کو شامل ہو اور ان تجمعوا بین الاختین بھی ہر قسم کی جمع کو شامل نہو تو معلوم ہوا کہ عام سر فرد پر موجب حکم ہوتا ہے
What your right hands possess is declared lawful because in this verse 'what your right hands possess' is a general term that indicates that intercourse with every owned female is lawful, whether she is one among sisters or alone. And the other verse declares combining two sisters as unlawful, i.e., 'and that you combine between two sisters' because this verse indicates that combining two sisters is unlawful whether through marriage or intercourse by right hand possession; all of this is unlawful. So the prohibitor is preponderant, meaning when there is conflict between permissibility and prohibition, the prohibitor is preponderant. As will come in the chapter of conflict, that the prohibitor is preponderant over the permissive. So this conflict can only occur when 'what your right hands possess' includes an owned woman and 'and that you combine between two sisters' also includes every kind of combination. So it is understood that a general term applies its ruling to every individual.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of two Quranic verses: one that generally permits intercourse with slave women (ma malakat aymanukum) and another that prohibits combining two sisters (an tajma'u bayn al-ukhtayn). The commentator explains that both verses are general in scope. The permitting verse applies to all owned women, even if they are sisters. The prohibiting verse applies to any form of combining sisters, whether through marriage or concubinage. When these general texts conflict, the principle is that the prohibitory text (muharram) takes precedence over the permissive (mubah). This is a Hanafi usul rule. The conflict arises only if the permitting text includes the case of sisters and the prohibiting text includes ownership. The resolution by tarjih shows that general terms apply to all individuals they cover, and when they conflict, one is given preponderance.

Key Terms
محرم (muharram): Prohibitory ruling; a text that declares something unlawful.
عام (amm): A word that covers all individuals of its category without restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Is 'Amm Qat'i or Zanni? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Usul dispute on definitiveness of general terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the definitiveness of general terms and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) for specification of Quranic 'amm by khabar wahid and qiyas.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold 'amm as qat'i until evidence of specification; Shafi'is hold it as zanni due to widespread specification.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: Is a general term ('amm) in the Quran and Sunnah definitive (qat'i) or probable (zanni)? The answer determines which sources can specify it. The Hanafi school, as argued in the text, maintains that 'amm is qat'i in the absence of specifiers, while the Shafi'is consider it zanni because specification is common. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from primary texts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi claim: 'amm is qat'i like khass (specific). This means the general term applies definitively to all its individuals unless there is clear evidence of specification.
The text says 'muthli khass'—just like the specific is definitive, so is the general.
2
State the Shafi'i position
Identify the Shafi'i claim: 'amm is zanni (prima facie). It carries a possibility of specification, which makes it non-definitive until proven otherwise.
The Shafi'i proof is that specification is widespread, even proverbial: 'There is no general except that some of it has been specified.'
3
Analyse the concept of 'possibility' (ihtimal)
Both schools agree that the possibility of specification does not arise from a proof (dalil) but from the nature of language. The Hanafis argue that only a possibility that stems from a proof can weaken definitiveness; a bare logical possibility does not affect it.
The text clarifies two meanings of qat'i: (1) no possibility at all, (2) no possibility from a proof. The Hanafis adopt the second.
4
Compare the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
Since Shafi'is consider 'amm zanni, they allow its specification by khabar wahid and qiyas. Hanafis require stronger evidence (like mutawatir or qat'i dalil) to specify a Quranic 'amm.
This affects rulings in many chapters, e.g., the permissibility of combining two sisters in marriage based on 'ma malakat aymanukum'.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view) in the text
The text presents the Hanafi view as the relied-upon position: 'And according to us, the general is definitive.'
The tarjih is implicit in the structure of the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك عام كله یعنی یہ نصوص اربعہ جن کے ساتھ حضرت علی اور ابن مسعود ض نے تمسک اختیار کیا ہے ۔ فی الجمع بین الاحتین کے بارہ میں یعنی ما ملکت ایمانکم اور ان تجمعوا بین الاختين والعدة اور عدت کے بارہ میں والدین بیتو فون اور اولات الاحمال یہ نصوص اربعہ عام ہیں جن کے ساتھ ان حضرات نے استدلال بالعموم کر کے ان کے

لكن عند الشافعي هو دلیل جوحضرات اس کے قائل تھے کہ عام جن افراد کو شامل ہوتا ہے ان تمام میں موجب حکم ہوتا ہے پھر ان حضرات کا آپس میں اختلاف ہے کہ اس کا موجب ظنی ہے یا قطعی ہے یعنی اس کے اندر خصوص کا احتمال ناشی عن الدلیل نہ ہو باقی رہا احتمال غير ناشي عن الدلیل وہ قطعیت کے منافی نہیں ہے امام شافعی ” قائل ہیں کہ اس کا موجب ظنی ہے۔ اسی کو پیش کرتے ہوئے کہا لکن عند الشافعی یعنی امام شافعی کے نزدیک یہ ایک اپنی دلیل ہے جس میں شہر ہے یعنی ظنی ہے لہذا اس کی تخصیص خبر واحد اور قیاس کے ساتھ جائز ہے یعنی کتاب اللہ میں جو عام واقع ہے اس کی تخصیص خبر واحد اور قیاس کے ساتھ ان کے نزدیک جائز ہے ۔ لان کل عام یہ دلیل شوافع ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ ہر عام احتمال تخصیصی رکھتا ہے اور تخصیص عام میں شائع ذائع اور کثیر الوقوع ہے حتی کہ بطور ضرب مثل کے مشہور ہو چکا ہے کہ " ما من عام الا وقد خص منہ البعض " ہاں بمعونتہ قرائن قطعی ہو جاتا ہے جس کے اندر احتمال تخصیص بالکل ختم ہو جاتا ہے جیسے ان اللہ بکل شئ علیم اور اللہ مافی السموات والارض - وعندنا هو اى العام قطعی مساء الخاص یعنی جیسے خاص قطعی ہے بالاتفاق اسی طرح عند الحنفیہ عام بھی قطعی ہے
وسیعی معنی القطعی قطعی دو معنی میں استعمال ہوتا ہے ایک یہ کہ جس کے اندر احتمال مطلقا نہ ہو نہ ناشی عن دلیل اور نہ ناشی بلا دلیل دوسرا معنی یہ ہے کہ اس کے اندر احتمال ناشی عن دلیل نہ ہو اور یہی معنی ثانی یہاں مراد ہے فلايجون تخصصہ یہ قطعی ہونے پر
And all that is general, meaning these four texts with which Hadrat 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them) sought support. Regarding combining two sisters: 'ma malakat aymanukum' and 'an tajma'u bayn al-ukhtayn'; and regarding 'iddah: 'wa alladhina yutawaffawna minkum' and 'ulaat al-ahmaal'. These four texts are general, with which they argued by the generality.

But according to al-Shafi'i, it is a proof. Those scholars held that the general term applies its ruling to all individuals it includes. Then they differed among themselves as to whether its implication is zanni (probable) or qat'i (definitive). That is, if it does not contain a possibility of specification arising from a proof, while the possibility not arising from a proof remains, that does not contradict definitiveness. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that its implication is zanni. Thus he said: 'But according to al-Shafi'i, it is a proof that is apparent (zanni).' Therefore specification of a general text by khabar wahid (solitary report) and qiyas is permissible. That is, a general term in the Book of Allah can be specified by khabar wahid and qiyas according to him. 'Because every general term...' This is the proof of the Shafi'is. In summary, every general term carries a possibility of specification, and specification of a general is widespread and frequent, so much so that it is proverbial: 'There is no general term except that some of it has been specified.' However, with the help of contextual evidence it becomes definitive, where the possibility of specification is completely eliminated, such as 'Indeed Allah is Knowing of all things' and 'His is what is in the heavens and the earth'. And according to us (Hanafis), the general is definitive like the specific. That is, just as the specific is definitively agreed upon, similarly according to the Hanafis the general is also definitive.

The meaning of 'qat'i' is used in two senses: one, that which has no possibility whatsoever, neither arising from a proof nor without proof; the second, that it has no possibility arising from a proof, and this second meaning is intended here. Therefore it cannot be specified because it is definitive.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding the nature of general terms ('amm). It begins by noting that the four texts discussed earlier (the two regarding combining sisters and the two regarding 'iddah) are all general, and the Companions argued by their generality. Then it presents the Shafi'i position: that 'amm is zanni (prima facie), not qat'i, and thus can be specified by solitary reports and analogy. The Shafi'i proof is that specification of general terms is widespread. However, they acknowledge that with strong contextual evidence, 'amm can become qat'i. The Hanafi position is that 'amm is qat'i like specific texts, meaning that if there is no evidence of specification, it is definitive. The text clarifies that 'qat'i' here means absence of possibility arising from a proof, not absolute impossibility. Therefore, according to Hanafis, a general text cannot be specified by mere khabar wahid or qiyas unless there is clear evidence from the source. This is a fundamental difference between the two schools in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
عام (amm): A word that includes all individuals of its category.
ظني (zanni): Probable, not definitive; allows for possibility of specification.
قطعي (qat'i): Definitive, leaving no room for contrary probability unless from evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers general terms ('amm) as definitive (qat'i) in the absence of specification evidence, whereas the Shafi'i school views them as probable (zanni) due to the widespread occurrence of specification, leading to different rules for permissible sources of specification.

Dispute on the Definitiveness of 'Amm in Hanafi and Shafi'i Usul
Refuting the Shafi'i view that 'amm is zanni and defending Hanafi position that it is qat'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the definitiveness of 'amm, explain the linguistic proof for the Hanafi view, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a scenario involving specification by khabar al-wahid.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'amm is qat'i until specified by qat'i evidence; Shafi'is treat it as zanni, allowing specification by zanni proofs.

This lesson dives into a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is: whether a general statement ('amm) in the Quran is definitively applicable to all its individuals (qat'i) or is speculative (zanni) and open to specification by weaker evidences. Understanding this difference is crucial for how each school derives rulings from general texts. We will examine the proofs from both sides, focusing on the Hanafi linguistic argument and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contested Issue
Determine what exactly is in dispute: whether 'amm in the Book of Allah is qat'i (definitive in its general application) or zanni (speculative, allowing specification by zanni proofs like khabar al-wahid or qiyas).
Both schools agree that 'amm can be specified, but disagree on the nature of its initial import.
2
State the Hanafi Position and Its Proof
Articulate the Hanafi view that 'amm is qat'i in the sense of general import (shumul) based on the principle of wad' (linguistic placement). The word is placed for its meaning, and generality is that meaning, so it is necessarily attached unless overridden by a qat'i evidence.
The Hanafi definition of qat'i here excludes probability arising from evidence, not all probability.
3
State the Shafi'i Position and Its Proof
Articulate the Shafi'i view that 'amm is zanni because every 'amm contains a probability of takhsis, and specification is widespread. Therefore, 'amm remains speculative until a qarinah makes it qat'i or specification occurs.
Shafi'is rely on the prevalence of takhsis and proverbial statements like 'No general except that some of it has been specified.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi Rebuttal
Understand how the Hanafis respond: non-evidential probability does not affect definitiveness. The probability of takhsis that is not supported by any evidence does not negate the qat'i nature of 'amm. Only after a first qat'i specification does 'amm become zanni.
This rebuttal uses a rational argument about trust in language and Shari'ah.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Apply the difference to a concrete scenario: whether a general Quranic verse (e.g., 'al-Sariqu wa al-Sariqatu' — cut the hand of thief) can be specified by a khabar al-wahid (e.g., 'no amputation for less than a dinar'). Hanafis require prior qat'i specification; Shafi'is allow it directly.
This step shows how the theoretical dispute affects actual istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ناسخ ہو گی لہذا حامل متوفی عنہاز و جہا کی عدت بروئے آیت ثانیہ وضع حمل ہوگی نہ کہ چار ماہ دس دن جو کہ اس عورت کے حق میں منسوخ سے اور وہ فرماتے تھے کہ من شاء باصلته جو شخص چاہے میں اس کے ساتھ مباحصلہ کرنے کے لئے تیار ہوں کہ نساء قصری کا نزول نسار طولی یعنی بقرہ کے بعد ہے لہذا آیت اولات الاحمال ناسخ ہوگی اور والذين یتو قون منکم بحق حاملہ منسوخ ہو گی ۔ وذلك عام کله یعنی یہ نصوص اربعہ جن کے ساتھ حضرت علی اور ابن مسعود ض نے تمسک اختیار کیا ہے ۔ فی الجمع بین الاحتین کے بارہ میں یعنی ما ملکت ایمانکم اور ان تجمعوا بین الاختين والعدة اور عدت کے بارہ میں والدین بیتو فون اور اولات الاحمال یہ نصوص اربعہ عام ہیں جن کے ساتھ ان حضرات نے استدلال بالعموم کر کے ان کے لكن عند الشافعي هو دلیل جوحضرات اس کے قائل تھے کہ عام جن افراد کو شامل ہوتا ہے ان تمام میں موجب حکم ہوتا ہے پھر ان حضرات کا آپس میں اختلاف ہے کہ اس کا موجب ظنی ہے یا قطعی ہے یعنی اس کے اندر خصوص کا احتمال ناشی عن الدلیل نہ ہو باقی رہا احتمال غير ناشي عن الدلیل وہ قطعیت کے منافی نہیں ہے امام شافعی ” قائل ہیں کہ اس کا موجب ظنی ہے۔ اسی کو پیش کرتے ہوئے کہا لکن عند الشافعی یعنی امام شافعی کے نزدیک یہ ایک اپنی دلیل ہے جس میں شہر ہے یعنی ظنی ہے لہذا اس کی تخصیص خبر واحد اور قیاس کے ساتھ جائز ہے یعنی کتاب اللہ میں جو عام واقع ہے اس کی تخصیص خبر واحد اور قیاس کے ساتھ ان کے نزدیک جائز ہے ۔ لان کل عام یہ دلیل شوافع ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ ہر عام احتمال تخصیصی رکھتا ہے اور تخصیص عام میں شائع ذائع اور کثیر الوقوع ہے حتی کہ بطور ضرب مثل کے مشہور ہو چکا ہے کہ " ما من عام الا وقد خص منہ البعض " ہاں بمعونتہ قرائن قطعی ہو جاتا ہے جس کے اندر احتمال تخصیص بالکل ختم ہو جاتا ہے جیسے ان اللہ بکل شئ علیم اور اللہ مافی السموات والارض - وعندنا هو اى العام قطعی مساء الخاص یعنی جیسے خاص قطعی ہے بالاتفاق اسی طرح عند الحنفیہ عام بھی قطعی ہے وسیعی معنی القطعی قطعی دو معنی میں استعمال ہوتا ہے ایک یہ کہ جس کے اندر احتمال مطلقا نہ ہو نہ ناشی عن دلیل اور نہ ناشی بلا دلیل دوسرا معنی یہ ہے کہ اس کے اندر احتمال ناشی عن دلیل نہ ہو اور یہی معنی ثانی یہاں مراد ہے فلايجون تخصصہ یہ قطعی ہونے پر تفریح ہے کہ عام کی تخصیص خبر واحد اور قیاس میں سے کسی ایک کے ساتھ جائز نہیں ۔ مالم شخص بقطعی یعنی جب تک اولاً اس کی تخصیص کسی قطعی دلیل کے ساتھ نہ ہو جائے اگر دلیل قطعی کے ساتھ عام کی تخصیص ایک مرتبہ ہو چکی ہے تو اب یہ ظنی ہو جائیں گا پھر اس کی تخصیص خبر واحد اور قیاس کے ساتھ ہو سکتی ہے لان اللفظ متنی وضع یہ قطعی ہونے کی دلیل خلاصہ یہ کہ لفظ جب کسی معنی کے لئے وضع کر دیا جائے تو یہ معنی اس کے لئے لازم غیر منفک ہو جاتا ہے جو اسی لفظ کے ساتھ ہمیشہ ثابت ہوتا رہتا ہے عند اطلاق اللفظ اور عموم بھی ایک ایسا معنی ہے کہ لفظ عام اس کیلئے وضع کیا گیا ہے لہذا یہ معنی اس لفظ موضوع کے لئے ہمیشہ لازم رسیگا اور عند اطلاق اللفظ الموضوع ہمیشہ ثابت ہوتا رہے گا ۔ الا ان تدل القرينة على خلافه الا یہ کہ کوئی قرینہ اور دلیل اس کے خلاف پر قائم ہو جائے تو اس دلیل کی وجہ سے خصوص مراد ہو سکتا ہے ۔ دلوجان ارادة البعض یہ مذہب مخالف کے بطلان پر دلیل ہے خلاصہ اس کا یہ ہے کہ اگر ارادہ بعض مسمیات عام کا از لفظ عام بلا قرینہ جائز ہو تو لارتفع الامان عن اللغة والشرع بالكلية يعنى اس صورت میں امان اور اعتماد لغت سے مرتفع اور ختم ہو جائیگا کیونکہ کلام عرب میں جو الفاظ عامہ وارد ہوئے ہیں اگر ان کے اندر احتمال خصوص موجود رہے تو پھر جو کچھ سامعین اس سے معنی عموم سمجھتے ہیں تو صحیح نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ ہر عام میں یہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ اس کا عمومی معنی مراد نہ ہو بلکہ اس کا کوئی خصوصی اور تخصیصی معنی مراد ہو تو اس صورت میں لغت پر اعتماد ختم ہو جائیگا اور اس صورت میں شریعت سے بھی اعتماد ختم ہو جائیگا کیونکہ اکثر خطابات شرعیہ عمومی ہیں جو خطاب عام کے ساتھ وارد ہیں ۔ اگر ارادہ بعض بلا قرینہ جائز ہو تو جو احکام عامہ صیغہ عموم سے سمجھتے ہیں وہ صحیح نہ ہو اور جو شخص مثلاً کہتا ہے کل عبدلی تصویر تو اس کے جمیع عبید کے عشق کا حکم صیحیح نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ ممکن ہے کہ اس سے بعض مراد ہوں تو یہ So it will be abrogating. Therefore, the 'iddah of a pregnant woman whose husband dies, by virtue of the second verse, is giving birth, not four months and ten days, which for this woman is abrogated. And he used to say: 'Whoever wishes, I am ready to debate with him' that the revelation of the short verses is after the long verses, i.e., after Surah al-Baqarah. Therefore, the verse 'Ulatu al-Ahmal' is abrogating, and 'Walladhina yutawaffawna minkum' for the pregnant woman is abrogated. And that is all general. That is, these four texts with which Hadrat Ali and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them) held firm concerning the combination between two sisters, i.e., 'Ma malakat aymanukum' and 'An tajma'u bayna al-ukhtain', and concerning 'iddah, 'Walladhina yutawaffawna' and 'Ulatu al-Ahmal'. These four texts are general, with which those honored ones argued by general application. But according to al-Shafi'i, it is a proof. Those honored ones believed that the general includes all individuals; it necessitates the ruling in all of them. Then among themselves they disagreed whether its import is speculative or definitive. That is, it does not contain a probability of specification arising from evidence; the remaining probability not arising from evidence does not contradict definitiveness. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that its import is speculative. Presenting this, he said: 'But according to al-Shafi'i', meaning that according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it is a proof of his, which has uncertainty, i.e., it is speculative. Therefore, its specification by khabar al-wahid and qiyas is permissible. That is, the general that occurs in the Book of Allah, its specification by khabar al-wahid and qiyas is permissible according to them. 'Because every general' is the proof of the Shafi'is. The summary is that every general contains a probability of specification, and specification of the general is widespread and frequent. So much so that it is famously said proverbially: 'There is no general except that some part of it has been specified.' Yes, by the help of conclusive indications it becomes definitive, in which the probability of specification is completely eliminated, like 'Indeed Allah is of all things Knowing' and 'To Allah belongs whatever is in the heavens and the earth.' And according to us, i.e., the general is definitive in the sense of the specific, just as the specific is definitively agreed upon, similarly according to the Hanafis the general is also definitive. The meaning of 'definitive' is used in two senses: one is that in which there is absolutely no probability, neither arising from evidence nor without evidence. The second meaning is that in which there is no probability arising from evidence, and this second meaning is intended here. Therefore, its specification is not permissible. This is a corollary of it being definitive: that specification of the general by khabar al-wahid or qiyas is not permissible. As long as it is not first specified by a definitive evidence. If the general has been specified once by a definitive evidence, then it will become speculative, and then its specification by khabar al-wahid and qiyas is possible. 'Because the word is placed for a meaning' – this is the proof of its definitiveness. The summary is that when a word is placed for a meaning, that meaning becomes necessary and inseparable for it, which remains established with that word always upon the utterance of the word. And generality is also a meaning for which the general word is placed. Therefore, this meaning will always be necessary for that placed word, and will always remain established upon the utterance of the placed word. Unless a contextual indication or evidence to the contrary appears, then by that evidence particularization may be intended. 'Indeed, intending some of the individuals' is a proof of the invalidity of the opposing school. Its summary is that if intending some of the individuals of the general from the general word without contextual indication were permissible, then trust in language and the Sacred Law would be completely removed. Because if the general words that occur in Arab speech contain a probability of specification, then what the listeners understand from them as general meaning would not be correct, because in every general it is possible that the general meaning is not intended, but rather some specific and particular meaning is intended. In that case, trust in language would be lost, and in that case trust in the Sacred Law would also be lost, because most of the sacred addresses are general, which occur with a general address. If intending some without contextual indication were permissible, then the general rulings that are understood from the general expression would not be correct. And if a person says, for example, 'Every slave of mine is freed', then the ruling of freedom for all his slaves would not be correct, because it is possible that some of them were intended. This would be obfuscation to the listener, which is invalid. And this invalidity necessarily follows from your considering the general as containing probability of specification and deeming intending some permissible. Therefore, this is invalid. As for the probability that does not arise from evidence, it has no consideration, and that kind of probability cannot affect the definitiveness of the general. And the probability of specification here: this kind of probability of specification in the general is like the probability of metaphor in the specific. So just as that probability of metaphor does not prevent the definitiveness of the specific, and

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shafi'i position on the nature of 'amm (general) in usul al-fiqh, and a defense of the Hanafi position that 'amm is qat'i (definitive). The author begins by referencing a previous discussion about the 'iddah of a pregnant widow, where Hadrat Ali and Ibn Mas'ud used four general texts (nusus arba'ah) to argue that the later verse (Ulatu al-Ahmal) abrogates the earlier one (Walladhina yutawaffawna), and that these texts are general and should be applied generally. Then the author states that according to Imam al-Shafi'i, these general texts are merely proofs that are zanni (speculative), not qat'i. The Shafi'i position is that every 'amm contains a probability of takhsis (specification), and therefore it can be specified by khabar al-wahid (isolated hadith) or qiyas (analogy). The author notes that the Shafi'is consider this kind of specification widespread, even proverbial: 'There is no general except that some part of it has been specified.' However, they admit that some verses like 'Inna Allaha bikulli shay'in 'alim' are qat'i by virtue of qara'in (indications) that eliminate any probability of specification. The author then presents the Hanafi position: 'amm is qat'i in the same way that khass (specific) is qat'i. He clarifies that 'qat'i' here means the second sense: it does not contain any probability arising from evidence, although a non-evidential probability may remain. This kind of probability does not affect definitiveness. Therefore, according to the Hanafis, 'amm cannot be specified by zanni proofs like khabar al-wahid or qiyas unless it has been first specified by a qat'i proof—after that first specification, it becomes zanni and can be further specified by zanni proofs. The core Hanafi proof is linguistic: words are placed for meanings, and the meaning of 'amm is generality (shumul). This meaning is necessarily attached to the word, so whenever the word is used, it conveys generality unless a clear qarinah (indication) overrides it. To allow the possibility of intending some individuals without any contextual indication would destroy trust in language altogether, because every general statement could be misinterpreted. This would also undermine the Shari'ah, since most of its addresses are general. The author gives an example: if someone says 'Kullu 'abdi li tahrir' (Every slave of mine is free), and it were possible that he meant only some, then the ruling would be ambiguous and unreliable. Thus, the Hanafis argue that 'amm is qat'i in its general application unless there is a specific qat'i evidence to specify it.

Key Terms
عام ('Amm): A word that encompasses all individuals of its category without limitation.
قطعی (Qat'i): Definitive: a meaning that has no probability arising from evidence, though non-evidential probability may remain.
ظنی (Zanni): Speculative: a meaning that has probability of the opposite, open to interpretation.
تخصیص (Takhsis): Specification: restricting a general ruling to some of its individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on specifying a general ('amm) verse of the Quran with a khabar al-wahid (isolated hadith)?
A) It is always permissible because 'amm is zanni.
B) It is never permissible under any circumstances.
C) It is permissible only if the 'amm has been first specified by a qat'i evidence, otherwise not.
D) It is permissible in matters of worship but not in transactions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'amm is qat'i based on linguistic placement, so it cannot be specified by zanni proofs until a first qat'i specification has occurred.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
The Qat'i Nature of 'Amm and Its Specification
Establishes that 'amm is qat'i and can only be specified by qat'i proof first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for specifying 'amm by khabar wahid or qiyas, and evaluate the distinction between possibilities that arise from a proof and baseless possibilities.

Core Idea
A general word is definitively binding until specified by a definite proof; only then can speculative proofs restrict it.

In Hanafi usul, the general word (‘amm) is considered definitive (qat‘i) in its indication until a clear proof restricts it. But what kind of proof can initially specify a general text? This lesson explores the rule that only a definitive proof (like a mutawatir text) can first specify an ‘amm, after which it becomes conjectural (zanni) and may be further specified by weaker proofs like khabar wahid or qiyas. The passage presents the Hanafi argument based on lexical assignment and refutes the possibility of unspecified partial intent.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule on takhsis of 'amm
The rule: a general word cannot be specified by khabar wahid or qiyas unless it has first been specified by a qat'i dalil. This is the core ruling expounded in the passage.
2
Trace the proof: lexical assignment (wad‘)
The passage argues that every word is placed for a meaning (wad‘), and that meaning becomes necessary and inseparable upon utterance. Since 'amm is placed for generality, that meaning is definitive unless a qarina (indication) overrides it.
This is a rational (aqli) proof, not naqli.
3
Distinguish between possibilities
The Hanafi school distinguishes between possibilities that arise from a proof (nashi’ ‘an al-dalil) and baseless possibilities (ghayr nashi’). Only possibilities supported by a proof affect definitiveness; the mere possibility of takhsis does not.
This is key to understanding why 'amm remains qat'i despite potential specification.
4
Contrast the opposing position
Opponents (Waqifiyya and Shafi‘i according to the text) argue that the possibility of specification makes 'amm inherently zanni. The Hanafi response is that such a possibility is baseless and does not undermine certainty.
The text compares this to the possibility of majaz in a specific word not affecting its definitiveness.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In fiqh, Hanafi scholars treat general Quranic verses as binding on all their instances unless a qat'i evidence specifies them. Shafi‘i scholars allow specification by khabar wahid without prior qat'i takhsis.
Example: 'awliya' al-mayyit' (guardians of the deceased) in inheritance.

نہیں آئیگا
It is a clarification that the specification of the general (al-‘amm) by a singular report (khabar wahid) or by analogy (qiyas) is not permissible. Unless the general is first specified by a definitive proof (qat‘i). That is, as long as it has not first been specified by a definitive proof. If the general has been specified once by a definitive proof, then it becomes conjectural (zanni). Then its specification by a singular report or analogy becomes possible. Because the word is placed (wad‘) for a meaning—this is the proof of its definitiveness. The summary: when a word is placed for a meaning, this meaning becomes a necessary inseparable attribute (lazim ghayr munfakk) which remains established with that word forever, upon the utterance of the word. And generality is also such a meaning that the general word is placed for it. Therefore this meaning will always remain necessary for that placed word, and upon utterance of the placed word it will always remain established, unless an indication (qarina) or proof indicates otherwise, so due to that proof a specific meaning can be intended. [As for] the point that intending some (of the individuals) is the proof of the invalidity of the opposing school: its summary is that if intending some individuals of the general from the general word without a qarina is permissible, then trust in language and revelation would be completely removed. That is, in that case trust and reliance upon language would be removed, because among the words of the Arabs that are general, if there remains within them the possibility of specification, then what the listeners understand from them as the general meaning would not be correct, because it is possible that in every general the general meaning is not intended but rather some specific meaning is intended. In that case, trust in language would be removed, and trust in revelation would also be removed because most legal addresses are general, having come with a general form. If intending some without a qarina is permissible, then the general rulings that are understood from the general form would not be correct. For example, if someone says 'Kullu ‘abdin li tahri' (every slave of mine is free), then the ruling of emancipation for all his slaves would not be correct, because it is possible that he intended some. This is delusion upon the hearer, which is invalid. And this invalidity necessarily follows from you making the general possible of specification, considering intending some permissible. So this is invalid. As for the possibility not arising from a proof, it has no consideration, and such possibility cannot affect the definitiveness of the general. The possibility of specification here: this kind of possibility of specification in the general is like the possibility of metonymy (majaz) in the specific (al-khass). Just as this possibility of metonymy does not create any difference in the definitiveness of the specific and its establishing of the named, because this possibility arises without proof, similarly, from this kind of possibility there will be no difference in the definitiveness of the general.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the Hanafi position on the definitiveness (qat‘i) of general words (‘amm) and their specification (takhsis). The author states the rule: a general word cannot be specified by conjectural evidences like khabar wahid or qiyas unless it has first been specified by a definitive proof (e.g., a mutawatir text). Once specified by a definitive proof, the word loses its qat‘i status and becomes zanni, allowing further specification by zanni proofs. The proof relies on the concept of lexicographical assignment (wad‘). Every word is assigned a meaning, and that meaning is necessarily attached to it whenever the word is uttered; generality is such an assigned meaning for general words. Thus, the general word always indicates all its individuals unless a qarina (indication) points otherwise. The author then rebuts the opposing view that allows specifying some individuals without a qarina. If that were allowed, language would become unreliable because one could never be sure that a general statement really means all individuals; this would also affect shari‘ah since most legal texts are general. An example is given: if someone says 'kullu ‘abdin li hurrun' (every slave of mine is free), without qarina, it must mean all; otherwise, it would be deception. The possibility that does not arise from a proof (ihtimal ghayr nashi’ ‘an al-dalil) is disregarded and does not affect definitiveness, just as the possibility of majaz in a specific word does not affect its definitiveness.

Key Terms
عام (‘amm): A word that, according to lexical assignment, is placed to cover all individuals of its category without restriction.
قطعیت (qat‘iyyah): Definitiveness; a property of a word's indication that leaves no room for contrary possibility unless supported by a proof.
تخصیص (takhsis): Restricting the general meaning of a word to some of its individuals by an evidence (dalil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what is the condition for specifying a general word ('amm) by a singular report or analogy?
A) It is always permissible without any condition.
B) It is permissible only after the general has been specified once by a definitive proof.
C) It is never permissible because 'amm is qat'i forever.
D) It is permissible if there is a possibility of specification.

Key Takeaway
A general word (‘amm) is definitively binding until a definitive proof specifies it; thereafter, it becomes conjectural and can be further specified by weaker proofs.

The Hanafi Response: Why 'Amm Is Qat'i Despite Possibility of Takhsis
Responds to Waqifiyya and Shafi'i objections regarding ta'kid and probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi argument for the qat'i status of 'amm in the face of objections from Waqifiyya and al-Shafi'i, and evaluate the distinction between possibilities arising from proof and those not arising from proof.

Core Idea
Emphasis (ta'kid) repels baseless possibilities but does not create definitiveness; 'amm is inherently qat'i.

In this passage, the Hanafi school defends the definitive (qat'i) nature of general texts ('amm) against two objections: that emphasis with 'kull' indicates lack of exhaustiveness, and that 'amm's possibility of specification makes it zanni. The Hanafi response hinges on the crucial distinction between possibilities that arise from a proof (nashi' 'an al-dalil) and those that do not. Only the former affect definitiveness. Emphasis removes the latter, bringing 'amm to the rank of muhkam (firm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
List the Waqifiyya claim (need for ta'kid shows non-exhaustiveness) and the Shafi'i claim (two possibilities make 'amm zanni).
These are the opposing positions that the Hanafi response addresses.
2
Distinguish types of possibility
Differentiate between possibility that arises from a proof (nashi' 'an al-dalil) and possibility that does not (ghayr nashi'). The Hanafi school argues that only the former affects definitiveness.
This distinction is central to the Hanafi defense.
3
Analyze the Hanafi analogy with khass
Examine the analogy between 'amm and khass: khass is qat'i despite possibility of majaz, and emphasis with 'nafs' and 'ayn' removes that baseless possibility; similarly, emphasis with 'kull' and 'ajma‘' for 'amm removes baseless possibilities, but does not create definitiveness.
The analogy shows that ta'kid is not needed for qat‘iyya.
4
Evaluate the counter-objection
Consider the claim that 'amm has two possibilities (majaz and takhsis) while khass has only one. The Hanafi response: takhsis is also a form of majaz, so both have two possibilities; multiplicity does not affect definitiveness if the possibilities do not arise from proof.
This refutes the idea that more possibilities make 'amm zanni.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: for Hanafi, 'amm is a definitive proof until specified; for Shafi'i, 'amm is zanni and requires verification before use in ijtihad.
This difference impacts how general Quranic commands are interpreted.

فالتكيد يجعله محکما یہ مخالفین کے تمسکات کا جواب ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ واقفیہ نے جو کہ قاتل بالتوقف تھے انھوں نے استدلال میں پیش کیا تھا کہ عام کی تاکید جو کل اور ا جمع کیساتھ کی جاتی ہے اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ عام فی حد دانہ مستغرق لجميع الافراد نہیں ہے تو اس کے جواب میں کہا کہ کل کے ساتھ تاکید اس لئے کی جاتی ہے کہ وہ عام محکم ہو جائے یعنی عام میں احتمال نانشی عن الدليل تو تھا نہیں البتہ غیر ناشی من الدلیل ہو سکتا تھا تو تاکید کے ساتھ ہر قسم کا احتمال مندفع ہو کر محکم کے درجہ تک پہونے جائے گا ۔ وايضا جواب عما قال الشافعي یعنی امام شافعی کے قول کہ التخصیص کا جواب بھی ہے حاصل یہ کہ ہم اس کے مدعی نہیں ہیں کہ عام میں احتمال تخصیص اصلا نہیں ہے نہ ناشی عن الدلیل نہ بغیر ناشی بلکہ ہم اس کے قائل ہیں کہ احتمال تخصیص ناشی عن الدلیل موجود نہیں ہے غیر ناشی عن الدلیل جو قطعیت کے منافی نہیں اس کی نفی کے ہم مدعی نہیں میں انسی غیر ناشی عن الدلیل کی نفی کے لیئے تاکید بکل اور اجمع کی جاتی ہے تاکہ محکم ہو جائے یہ ایسے سمجھے جیسے خاص بالاتفاق قطعی ہے لیکن اس کے اندر احتمال مجاز ہے تو اس احتمال کو دفع کرنے کے لئے تاکید بلفظ نفس وعین کی جاتی ہے کہا جاتا ہے جانی زید نفسه وعینہ تاکہ احتمال مجاز مندفع ہو جائے جیسے اس تاکید کے ساتھ خاص کی قطعیت میں فرق نہیں آتا اسی طرح کل اور اجمع کی تاکید کے ساتھ عام کی قطعیت میں فرق نہیں آئیں گا ۔ فان قيل احتمال المجاز الذى اخر خلاصہ سوال یہ ہے کہ عام مساوی للخاص في القطعیت نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ خاص میں صرف احتمال مجاز ہے اور عام میں یہی احتمال مجاز بھی ثابت ہے اور اس کے علاوہ احتمال آخر یعنی احتمال تخصیص بھی ثابت ہے تو خاص میں ایک احتمال ہے اور عام میں دو فيكون الخاص راجحاً تو خاص کو ترجیح علی العام حاصل ہے تو خاص بدرجہ نص ہوا اور عام بمنزلہ ظاہر - نص کو ترجیح علی الظاہر حاصل ہے قلنا لما كان العام التم خلاصہ جواب یہ کہ عام کی وضع چونکہ برائے کل مسمیات ہے تو اس سے بعض کا ارادہ کرنا اور بعض کا نہ کرنا جس کو آپ تخصیص کہہ رہے ہیں یہ بھی تو مجاز ہے تو حاصل یہ ہوا کہ خاص میں ایک احتمال مجاز ہوا اور عام میں دو احتمال مجاز ہو گئے اور کثرۃ احتمالات کا کوئی اعتبا
Then emphasis (ta'kid) makes it firm (muhkam). This is the reply to the objections of the opponents. Summary: The Waqifiyyah, who were those who suspended judgment, presented in their argument that the emphasis of the general with 'kull' and 'ajma‘' indicates that the general is not inherently exhaustive of all individuals. In response, it is said: emphasis with 'kull' is done to make the general firm (muhkam), i.e., the general did not have a possibility arising from a proof, but it could have a possibility not arising from a proof. So with emphasis, every kind of possibility is repelled and it reaches the level of muhkam. Also, a reply to what al-Shafi‘i said: that is, this is also a reply to Imam al-Shafi‘i’s statement about specification. The gist: we do not claim that the general has absolutely no possibility of specification—neither arising from a proof nor not arising. Rather, we hold that the possibility of specification arising from a proof does not exist. As for the possibility not arising from a proof, which is not contrary to definitiveness, we do not deny its existence. To negate this non-arising possibility, emphasis with 'kull' and 'ajma‘' is used so that it becomes muhkam. This is understood like the specific (al-khass) is definitively (qat‘i) by agreement, but it has the possibility of metonymy (majaz). To repel this possibility, emphasis with the words 'nafs' and 'ayn' is used; it is said 'ja'a Zaydun nafsuhu wa ‘aynuhu' (Zayd himself came) so that the possibility of majaz is repelled. Just as with this emphasis, no difference comes in the definitiveness of the specific, similarly with the emphasis of 'kull' and 'ajma‘', no difference will come in the definitiveness of the general. But if it is said: The possibility of majaz that... Summary of the objection: The general cannot be equal to the specific in definitiveness, because the specific only has the possibility of majaz, while the general has this same possibility of majaz plus another possibility, namely the possibility of specification. So the specific has one possibility, and the general has two possibilities. Therefore the specific is preponderant (rajih). So the specific is at the level of nass (clear text), and the general is at the level of zahir (apparent). The nass has preponderance over the zahir. We said: Because the general... Summary of the reply: Since the placement (wad‘) of the general is for all individuals, intending some and not others (which you call specification) is also a kind of majaz. So the outcome: the specific has one possibility of majaz, and the general has two possibilities of majaz. And multiplicity of possibilities has no consideration... (text incomplete)
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage contains two responses to objections regarding the definitiveness of 'amm. First, the Waqifiyyah (those who suspend judgment) argued that because 'amm is sometimes emphasized with words like 'kull' (all) and 'ajma‘' (entirely), it shows that without emphasis, 'amm does not necessarily indicate all individuals. The Hanafi response: Emphasis is used to repel any residual possibility (especially those not arising from proof) and to raise 'amm to the rank of muhkam (firm). It does not mean that without emphasis 'amm is not qat‘i; rather emphasis removes even baseless possibilities to achieve the highest clarity. Second, the response to Imam al-Shafi‘i: Al-Shafi‘i allegedly held that 'amm inherently contains a possibility of specification, so it is zanni. The Hanafi reply distinguishes between possibilities that arise from a proof (nashi’ ‘an al-dalil) and those that do not. Only the former affect definitiveness. The latter, like the possibility of majaz in a specific word, are disregarded. Emphasis with 'kull' and 'ajma‘' removes even the baseless possibility, similar to emphasizing 'nafs' and 'ayn' for specific words to remove the possibility of majaz. Then a further objection: Even so, 'amm has two possibilities (majaz and takhsis) while khass has only one (majaz), so khass is more definitive. The answer: Both possibilities in 'amm are actually possibilities of majaz, because takhsis is a form of majaz (using a word for part of its meaning). So both have two possibilities of majaz, but the number does not affect definitiveness; the key is whether possibilities arise from proof. The text cuts off here but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
تأکید (ta'kid): Emphasis using words like 'kull' and 'ajma‘' to remove any residual possibility and make the general meaning firm (muhkam).
محکم (muhkam): The highest level of textual clarity where no possibility of interpretation (ta'wil) remains.
مجاز (majaz): Metonymical or figurative use of a word. Here, the possibility that a word is used figuratively, which does not negate its literal definitiveness if no proof supports that possibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why does emphasis with 'kull' or 'ajma‘' not prove that 'amm is inherently non-exhaustive?
A) Because emphasis is used for other rhetorical purposes.
B) Because emphasis removes only possibilities that do not arise from a proof, not because 'amm was zanni without it.
C) Because emphasis changes the meaning of 'amm completely.
D) Because emphasis is optional and has no legal effect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'amm is qat'i by default; emphasis only removes baseless possibilities, and the multiplicity of possibilities does not affect definitiveness unless supported by proof.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
